Defining a City for Jumu'ah (Misr Jami') in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines the criteria for a city to host Jumu'ah and the sufficiency of a judge/mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi definition of a misr jami' and explain why a judge or mufti suffices for the requirement of multiple persons (ta'dad).

Core Idea
Jumu'ah requires a city; the Hanafi definition is functional—governance and justice, not mere population count.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explores what constitutes a valid city (misr jami') for the Friday prayer. While many definitions exist among Hanafi scholars, the commentary settles on a functional description attributed to Abu Hanifa. Additionally, it addresses the sufficiency of a judge or mufti to fulfill the requirement of multiple persons, emphasizing knowledge of rulings over numerical quantity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the validity of Jumu'ah depends on the location being a misr jami'. The passage collects multiple opinions on its definition.
The core of the discussion is a condition (shart) for Jumu'ah.
2
List the various definitions mentioned
Scan the passage for all cited criteria: buildings like Mina's, presence of religious/worldly needs, market, ruler, scholar, physician, population thresholds, etc.
These represent intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Identify the most correct (asahh) definition
Note that the commentary declares Abu Hanifa's definition as the most correct: a large town with streets, markets, surrounding villages, and a governor who can enforce justice.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Explain the sufficiency of a judge/mufti for ta'dad
Understand that if the judge or amir is a mufti (qualified to issue fatwas), that fulfills the requirement of multiple persons because the purpose is knowledge of rulings, not a specific number.
This shows the school's emphasis on functional qualification.
5
Apply the definition to practical scenarios
Consider modern towns: a small village without a governing authority and no market would not qualify; a town with a functioning judge and regular market would.
This clarifies the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And its buildings reach the buildings of Mina, according to the apparent riwayah. And if the judge or the amir is a mufti, it suffices for the multiple persons. The [matter of] hudud, as in al-'Inayah, and he sufficed with mentioning the hudud instead of qisas; because whoever has the power to implement them owns them, as in al-Fath. And the state is that the place, its buildings reach the size of the buildings of Mina, and this is according to the apparent riwayah: Qadi Khan said it, and upon it is reliance, as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah from al-Khulasah, and in Majma' al-Riwayat: And he said in al-Mustasfa: The best of what has been said about it is: if there are found in it the necessities of religion, i.e., the judge, the mufti, and the sultan, and there are found in it all worldly necessities, then it is a misr jami', otherwise not, said Fakhr al-Islam rahimahullah. And in al-Tahdhib: And it was said: what has a running market, a mighty sultan, a scholar, a skilled physician, and in al-Muhit: it is jami'. And among the sayings: it is that if its people gather in their largest mosque, it does not contain them; and among them: that ten thousand people reside in it; and among them: that it contains ten thousand fighters, besides the elders and children, and among them a scholar, and professionals whose profession is needed, and the governor and judge implement hudud in it; and among them: that every craftsman lives by his craft from year to year, not needing to switch from one craft to another; and among them: that it is every place that the imam makes a city, as if he sends to a village a deputy for implementing hudud and a judge, then when he dismisses him it returns to being a village; and among them: that it is every place whose people have such strength and power that if an enemy approaches them, they repel him from themselves; and among them: that every day a child is born in it and a person dies; and among them: that its population is not known except with effort and difficulty; and among them: what is narrated from Abu Hanifa: it is a large town with streets and markets and has villages (rasatiq), and in it a governor who can give justice to the oppressed from the oppressor by his retinue and knowledge or knowledge of others, and people refer to him regarding incidents that occur to them, and this is the most correct, as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah, and it is like what we mentioned in the matn except that he did not specify the judge, (and if the judge or the amir is a mufti, it suffices for the multiple persons) because the criterion is knowledge of rulings, not multiplicity of persons.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement from the commentary. It begins with the matn lines stating that for a city to be valid for Jumu'ah, its buildings must reach the size of Mina's buildings—this is according to the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah). Additionally, if the judge or the amir is a mufti (qualified to issue fatwas), that fulfills the requirement of multiple persons (ta'dad). The commentary then expands on the definition of a 'misr jami'' (city fit for Jumu'ah) by listing numerous opinions from earlier Hanafi authorities. These include conditions such as the presence of religious necessities (judge, mufti, sultan) and worldly necessities, a market, a strong ruler, a scholar, a physician, a population of ten thousand, and the ability to defend against enemies. The relied-upon definition, attributed to Abu Hanifa, is that it is a large town with streets, markets, and surrounding villages, with a governor who can enforce justice, and people refer to him for their legal needs. The commentary then reaffirms the matn's ruling on the judge/mufti, stating that the criterion is the knowledge of rulings, not the number of persons. This shows that the Hanafi school prioritizes functional qualifications over numerical quantity.

Key Terms
مصر جامع (misr jami'): A city large enough for Jumu'ah; defined by various criteria, the most correct being Abu Hanifa's: a large town with streets, markets, surrounding villages, and a governor who enforces justice.
التعداد (al-ta'dad): The requirement of multiple persons for Jumu'ah; here, a judge or amir who is a mufti suffices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is a 'misr jami' (city valid for Jumu'ah)?
A) A city with exactly ten thousand inhabitants.
B) A large town with streets, markets, surrounding villages, and a governor who can enforce justice.
C) Any place with a mosque and a market.
D) A city with a judge and a mufti only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on a functional definition of a city for Jumu'ah, focusing on governance and justice rather than population numbers.

Jumu'ah in Mina During the Pilgrimage Season: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on performing Jumu'ah in Mina for the caliph or amir of Hajj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of Jumu'ah in Mina during Hajj and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Jumu'ah in Mina is valid only for the caliph or amir of al-Hijaz, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees.

This passage from Sharh Nur al-Idah presents a well-known ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school regarding Jumu'ah during the Hajj season. The core question is whether Mina's temporary urbanization (tatamassar) during the pilgrimage justifies the establishment of the Friday prayer. Understanding this dispute refines your ability to analyze the interplay between spatial rulings and social circumstances in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling as stated by the matn: Jumu'ah is valid (jazat) in Mina for the caliph or amir of al-Hijaz.
Note that the amir of the season (Hajj official) is excluded.
2
Identify the opponent's view
Muhammad al-Shaybani rejects validity because Mina is a village (qarya).
He cites that 'Id prayer is not repeated in Mina, indicating it is not a city.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that Mina temporarily becomes a city (tatamassar) due to the large congregation, and the lack of a building is for ease of pilgrims.
They distinguish Mina from 'Arafat, which remains an open space and thus cannot host Jumu'ah.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: those in Mina during Hajj must perform Jumu'ah (if led by the proper authority) according to Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf; according to Muhammad, they pray Zuhr.
This affects the obligation of attendance for pilgrims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And Jumu'ah is valid in Mina, during the season, for the caliph or the amir of al-Hijaz, not the amir of the season, because he oversees only the affairs of the pilgrims according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: It is not valid in it because it is from the villages, and therefore one does not repeat the 'Id prayer in it. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), it becomes like a city during the days of the season, and the lack of restriction (to a building) is for ease, due to their preoccupation with the affairs of Hajj, unlike 'Arafat because it is an open space, so Jumu'ah is not established there, and prayer is not conditioned to be in a mosque in the town, so it is valid in an open space in it, just as if one prayed in his palace. Then he began to explain the extent of the obligation...
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding Jumu'ah in Mina during the Hajj season. The matn states that Jumu'ah is valid in Mina during the season for the caliph or the amir of al-Hijaz (the ruler of the region), not the amir of the season (who only manages Hajj rituals). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold this view. Their reasoning is that Mina temporarily becomes a city (tatamassar) during the season due to the large congregation, and the lack of a permanent building is for the ease of the pilgrims. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, arguing that Mina is a village (qarya) and therefore Jumu'ah is not valid there; he notes that the 'Id prayer is not repeated in Mina, indicating it is not a city. The commentary then distinguishes Mina from 'Arafat, which remains an open space and thus cannot host Jumu'ah. It also clarifies that prayer does not require a mosque; it can be performed in an open space within the town, similar to praying in one's palace.

Key Terms
أمير الحجاز (amir al-Hijaz): The ruler of the Hijaz region, who has authority over Mina; distinguished from 'amir al-mawsim' who only manages Hajj rituals.
تتمصر (tatamassar): Becomes like a city; temporary urbanization due to the large gathering for Hajj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because Mina temporarily becomes a city due to the large gathering, and ease for pilgrims.
C) Because the Prophet established Jumu'ah there.
D) Because 'Arafat is also valid for Jumu'ah.

Key Takeaway
The validity of Jumu'ah in Mina during Hajj depends on the authority of the caliph or amir of al-Hijaz and the temporary urbanization of Mina, per the preferred Hanafi view.

Minimum Friday Sermon: A Single Tasbih Suffices but is Disliked
Ruling with proof on minimum khutbah and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the minimum khutbah, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and apply the evidence (Quran and 'Uthman's story) to derive the ruling.

Core Idea
Any dhikr of Allah suffices for the Friday khutbah, but abandoning the sunnah is disliked.

The Friday khutbah is a central ritual, but what is its absolute minimum? This lesson explores the Hanafi position that a single tasbih or tahmid fulfills the obligation, though with strong dislike for leaving the prophetic practice. We will also examine the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two senior students, and the proofs each side uses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Dislike
The matn states that limiting the khutbah to a tasbih or tahmid is valid (sahih) but disliked (makruh). Note that validity does not mean the act is free of blame.
The dislike is tanzihi, not tahrimi.
2
Contrast with the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require a longer khutbah, at least the length of the tashahhud, because they consider the name 'khutbah' to imply a substantial discourse. Understand their reasoning and why they reject a minimal dhikr.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the Quranic Proof (Dalil Naqli)
Abu Hanifa uses the verse 'fas'aw ila dhikrillah' (al-Jumu'ah:9). The word 'dhikr' is general and includes any remembrance. Determine how this proves that a short tasbih qualifies, and why it does not restrict to a longer form.
The dalala is zanni because 'dhikr' is general but the Prophet's practice shows a preferred meaning.
4
Examine the Historical Evidence (Consensus of Companions)
The story of 'Uthman's first sermon: he only said 'al-hamdu lillah' and then some words. No companion objected, indicating either that a short speech is sufficient or that such a short phrase is linguistically a khutbah. Use this as evidence for Abu Hanifa's view.
This is cited as ijma suquti (tacit consensus).
5
Distinguish between Fard and Sunnah in the Khutbah
Abu Hanifa holds that the fard is any dhikr; the longer form is sunnah (or wajib according to some). Recognize that the Prophet's consistent practice does not make the longer form a condition for validity, because the Quranic command is not ambiguous.
The commentary emphasizes that the Qur'an is not mujmal, so the Prophet's action is not a bayan of an ambiguous command.

So he said regarding the khutbah: (and it is valid to limit the khutbah to) pure remembrance of Allah Almighty (like a tasbih, or a tahmid) or a tahlil or a takbir, but (with dislike) for abandoning the sunnah according to Abu Hanifa rahimahullah. He said: 'Indeed, Abu Bakr and 'Umar used to prepare speech for this occasion, and you are more in need of an effective imam than a talkative imam. Sermons will come to you later. I ask Allah the Great for forgiveness for myself and for you.' Then he descended and prayed with them. And none of them criticized him, so there was consensus from them, either on its not being a condition, or on that something like 'Praise be to Allah' is called a khutbah linguistically even if it is not so called customarily. And 'urtooj with tashdid (?) is correct: i.e., the khutbah became difficult for him and he could not complete it. And 'Uthman's intention was that those who come after the rightly-guided caliphs will have much talk and evil deeds, so I was not like them. I am on good not evil. He did not intend to prefer himself over the two shaykhs, as in al-Bahr and others. And all the conditions that are in the person praying and outside him are derived from the wording and indication of His statement: 'O you who believe, when the call is made for prayer on the day of Jumu'ah, hasten to the remembrance of Allah' [al-Jumu'ah:9], and all are in al-Mustasfa explained. (And the sunnas of the khutbah) that are in the khatib himself and that are in the khutbah itself are eighteen things approximately because they can be added to. As in al-Bahr from al-Hawi al-Qudsi. Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage discusses the minimum requirement for the Friday sermon (khutbah) and then begins to list its sunnas. It starts with the fragment 'yusamma' (called), likely continuing from the previous page. The matn states that it is valid to limit the khutbah to a single tasbih (glorification) or tahmid (praise of Allah), but this is disliked because it abandons the sunnah. Abu Hanifa holds this view. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require a longer khutbah, at least the length of the tashahhud including praise, salawat, and supplication, because the khutbah is wajib and a single tasbih is not considered a khutbah. The commentary then cites al-Tatarkhaniyyah that the first khutbah has four obligatory (fard) components: praise, salawat, enjoining taqwa, and recitation of a verse; the second has the same except supplication replaces verse recitation. Abu Hanifa's proof is the Quranic verse 'hasten to the remembrance of Allah' (Surah al-Jumu'ah:9), which uses the general term 'dhikr' (remembrance) without specifying length. Thus, any dhikr suffices technically, but the Prophet's consistent practice of delivering a longer khutbah makes the longer form sunnah or wajib (depending on interpretation). The story of 'Uthman is cited as evidence: when he became caliph, he gave a very short khutbah (just 'al-hamdu lillah' and some words) and none of the companions objected, indicating consensus that either a longer khutbah is not a condition or that such a short speech is linguistically a khutbah. The passage then transitions to the sunnas of the khutbah, stating they are approximately eighteen, and begins with the first: the khatib should sit in his private chamber on the right side of the minbar before ascending.

Key Terms
خطبة (khutbah): The Friday sermon; its minimum requirement is any dhikr of Allah (tasbih, tahmid, etc.) according to Abu Hanifa, but with dislike.
تسبيحة (tasbihah): Saying 'subhanallah'; a minimal form of dhikr that fulfills the khutbah.
سنة (sunnah): Recommended practice; in this context, the longer khutbah is sunnah, not fard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Any remembrance of Allah, such as saying 'subhanallah'.
C) Recitation of a verse from the Quran.
D) The khutbah must be at least the length of the tashahhud.

Key Takeaway
The Friday khutbah is valid with any dhikr of Allah, but the longer form is the sunnah and should be followed except in necessity.

Minimum Content of the Friday Khutbah: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
States the minimal valid khutbah and presents the intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the minimal valid khutbah, explain Abu Hanifa's dalil from the Quran and the story of Uthman, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Any dhikr suffices for the Friday khutbah; a longer sermon is sunnah, not a condition.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: how much must the Friday khutbah contain? The matn states that even a single tasbih is valid but disliked. Abu Hanifa holds that any remembrance of Allah fulfills the wajib, while the Sahibayn require a substantial sermon. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school balances textual generality (umum) with prophetic practice (sunnah) and consensus (ijma).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in the matn
Read the matn statement: 'And it is valid to limit the khutbah to something like a tasbihah or tahmidah, with dislike.' This is the base ruling: validity with karahah.
Note the phrase 'with dislike' indicates it is not the preferred practice.
2
State the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa says any dhikr suffices. The Sahibayn say a longer utterance, conventionally called a khutbah, is necessary. Identify the minimum each requires.
The Sahibayn's minimum is the length of tashahhud with praise, salutation, and supplication.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's primary dalil
He cites Quran 62:9: 'So hasten to the remembrance of Allah (dhikr).' The word 'dhikr' is general, so any remembrance fulfills the command.
The imperative indicates obligation, but the object is unrestricted in length.
4
Evaluate the supporting evidence from Uthman
The story of Uthman ascending the minbar and saying only 'Al-hamdu lillah,' then being unable to continue, yet no one objected. This indicates consensus (ijma) that such brevity is permissible.
The commentator clarifies Uthman's intent and that the short utterance was accepted.

الأعم بالدليل القاطع، غير أن المأثور عنه له اختيار أحد الفردين، أعني: الذكــر المسمى بالخطبة والمواظبة عليه، فكان ذلك واجباً أو سنة، لا أنه الشرط الذي لا يجزئ غيره، إذ لا يكون بياناً لأن الدليل وهو لفظ الذكر المأمور بالسعي إليه، ليس مجملاً ليقع فعله صلى الله عليه وسلم بيانــا للمجمل فلم يكن فرضاً، تنزيلاً للمشروعات على حسب أدلتها، ويؤيده قصة عثمان له أنـه لـمـا خـطـب أول جمعة [ ولي الخلافة صعد المنبر ] فقال: « الحمد الله فأرتج عليه، فقال: إن أبا بكر وعمر كانا يعدان لهذا المقام مقالاً، وأنتم إلى إمام فعّال أحوج منكم إلى إمام قوال، وستأتيكم الخطب بعد، واستغفر الله العظيم لي ولكم، ونزل وصلى بهم ) ()، ولم ينكر عليه أحد منهم، فكان إجماعاً منهم، إما على عدم اشتراطها، وإما على كون نحو الحمد الله يسمى خطبة لغة وإن لم يسم به عرفاً، وأُرتــج بالتخفيف على الأصح أي: استغلق عليه الخطبة فلم يقدر على إتمامها، ومراد عثمان له أن الذين يأتون بـعـد الخلفاء الراشدين يكونون على كثرة المقال وقبيح الفعال فأنا لم أكن مثلهم، فأنــا عـلـى الخير دون الشر، ولم يرد [ تفضيل نفسه ] ) على الشيخين كذا في [ البحر ] وغيره، وجملة الشروط التي في ذات المصلي والتي خارجة عنه تقتبس بالعبارة والإشارة من قوله تعالى: ﴿ يَتَأيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا إِذَا نُودِيَ للصلوةِ مِن يَوْمِ الْجُمُعَةِ فَاسْعَوْا إِلَى ذِكْرِ اللهِ ﴾ [ال : ٩] وكلها في «المستصفى»، مبينة
And it is valid to limit the khutbah to something like a tasbihah or tahmidah, with dislike (karahah). The sunan of the khutbah are eighteen things... the khutbah: (And it is valid to limit the khutbah to) pure remembrance of Allah Almighty (like a tasbihah or tahmidah) or tahlilah or takbirah, but (with dislike) due to abandoning the sunnah according to Abu Hanifah. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: A long utterance called a khutbah is necessary, and its minimum is the amount of the tashahhud up to his saying 'His slave and His messenger', containing praise, salutation, and supplication for the Muslims, because the khutbah is obligatory, and a tasbihah or tahmidah is not called a khutbah. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah from al-Sighnaqi: In the first khutbah there are four obligations: praising Allah, sending salutation, exhortation to fear Allah, and reciting a verse; similarly in the second, except that supplication in the second replaces the recitation of a verse in the first, as in Sharh al-Maqdisi. End. Abu Hanifah's proof is the saying of Allah: 'So hasten to the remembrance of Allah' without distinction between whether it is a long remembrance called a khutbah or not. Thus the condition is general remembrance (dhikr) by decisive evidence, but what is transmitted from him (the Prophet) is the choice of one of the two types, namely the remembrance called khutbah and being constant upon it; so that is wajib or sunnah, not that it is the condition that nothing else suffices, since that would not be a clarification because the evidence—the word 'remembrance' commanded to hasten to—is not mujmal such that his (the Prophet's) action would be a clarification of a mujmal, so it is not fard; this is applying the rulings according to their evidences. This is supported by the story of Uthman when he delivered the first Friday khutbah after assuming the caliphate, he ascended the minbar and said: 'Al-hamdu lillah' and then he was stuck (urtija 'alayhi). He said: 'Abu Bakr and Umar used to prepare speech for this occasion, but you need a doer more than a sayer; speeches will come to you; I seek forgiveness of Allah for myself and for you.' Then he descended and led them in prayer. None of them objected to him, so this was a consensus from them, either on not making the khutbah conditional or on considering something like 'al-hamdu lillah' a khutbah linguistically even if not customarily. 'Urtija' with tashdid is the correct version, i.e., the khutbah became stuck on him and he could not complete it. Uthman's intent was that those who come after the rightly guided caliphs will be abundant in speech and evil in action, so I am not like them; I am on good not evil; he did not intend to prefer himself over the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar). This is in al-Bahr and others. And the totality of conditions in the one praying and outside him are derived by explicit text and indication from His saying: 'O you who believe, when the call is made for prayer on Friday, hasten to the remembrance of Allah.' All of them are explained in al-Mustasfa.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the minimal content of the Friday khutbah. The matn states that it is valid to limit the khutbah to a single tasbih or tahmid, but with karahah (dislike) for abandoning the sunna. Abu Hanifa holds that any dhikr of Allah suffices, based on the Quranic command 'hasten to the remembrance of Allah' (dhikr), which is general. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require a substantial utterance that is conventionally called a khutbah, with a minimum similar to the tashahhud (including praise, salutation, and supplication). Abu Hanifa argues that the word 'dhikr' in the verse is not mujmal (ambiguous), so the Prophet's practice of a longer khutbah does not make that the only valid form; rather, it establishes the sunna but not a condition. He also cites the story of Uthman, who delivered a very short khutbah (just 'al-hamdu lillah') and the Companions did not object, indicating consensus that either such brevity is permissible or that such words are linguistically called a khutbah. The commentator clarifies that the khutbah is not like prayer; it can be delivered without facing the qibla and is not invalidated by speech. This is a Zahir al-Riwayah position from Abu Hanifa, though some later sources mention obligations within the khutbah.

Key Terms
ذكر (dhikr): Remembrance of Allah, including phrases like tasbih, tahmid, tahlil, takbir, and also extended sermons.
خطبة (khutbah): The sermon of Jumu'ah, which is wajib. Its minimum is disputed: any dhikr vs. a conventional sermon.
تسبيحة (tasbihah): An utterance of 'Subhanallah' or similar, considered a minimal form of dhikr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What evidence does Abu Hanifa primarily use to support his position that any dhikr suffices for the Friday khutbah?
A) A hadith stating that the khutbah can be brief.
B) The Quranic verse 'hasten to the remembrance of Allah' and the story of Uthman's short khutbah.
C) The consensus of the four imams.
D) An analogy with the call to prayer.

Key Takeaway
The Friday khutbah is valid with any dhikr of Allah according to Abu Hanifa, though a conventional sermon is the sunnah; the Sahibayn require a substantial utterance.

Sunan of the Friday Khutbah
Enumerates and explains the recommended practices of the khutbah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the sunan of the Friday khutbah, differentiate between sunnah and condition, and state the Hanafi position on purification during the khutbah.

Core Idea
The sunan of the khutbah are recommended acts; omitting them does not invalidate the khutbah.

The Friday khutbah is a wajib act, but many of its specific practices are recommended (sunnah). This passage enumerates approximately eighteen such practices, some related to the khatib and others to the khutbah itself. Understanding which acts are sunnah and which are conditions is crucial for correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the enumerated sunan
Read the list of approximately eighteen items. Note that the text says 'تقريباً' (approximately), allowing for additions.
The list includes purification, covering awrah, sitting on minbar, standing, leaning on sword, facing people, etc.
2
Classify each sunnah
Distinguish between sunan related to the khatib (e.g., dress, sitting) and those related to the khutbah itself (e.g., purification, content).
Some items like wearing black are for the khatib; purification is for the khutbah state.
3
Determine hukm level
All are sunnah muakkadah according to the majority. Note the exception of taharah: Abu Yusuf considered it a condition, but the mufta bihi is sunnah.
The text cites al-Dirayah, Majma' al-Riwayat, and al-Mustasfa to support the sunnah view.
4
Distinguish sunnah from condition
Understand that omitting a sunnah is makruh tanzih but does not invalidate; a condition would invalidate. The khutbah is not like prayer so many prayer conditions are not required.
Khutbah can be delivered without facing qibla, without silence, and without purity.
5
Apply the rulings in practice
Consider a scenario: a khatib delivers without wudu, facing away from people, leaning on a stick. Use the text to determine validity and disliked acts.
Overall khutbah valid but disliked; no condition broken.

And the sunan of the khutbah, which are in the khatib himself and in the khutbah itself, are approximately eighteen things because further items can be added. As in al-Bahr from al-Hawi al-Qudsi: It is sunnah for the khatib to sit in his chamber, to the right of the minbar; if there is none, then in its direction or side. He should wear black, following the caliphs, and because it is a tradition in every age and region. It is disliked for him to pray in the mihrab before the khutbah. End. It is recommended to wear white and black absolutely, so black is not exclusively required. Among the sunan is purification (taharah) during the khutbah, because of tradition; purification is not a condition for it because it is dhikr, and the junub and the one with minor impurity are not prevented from it. The khutbah is not like prayer, nor like half of it, evidenced by the fact that it can be performed facing other than the qibla, and speech does not invalidate it. The interpretation of the hadith that it is like half of prayer is in terms of reward, not in requiring all the conditions. But it is recommended that the junub repeat his khutbah as recommended to repeat his adhan. This is in al-Dirayah. In Majma' al-Riwayat: If he delivers the khutbah without purity, it is permissible but disliked. In al-Mustasfa: The correct view is that the khutbah does not take the place of half of prayer; the hadith refers to reward. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf that purification is a condition. And covering the private parts (satr al-awrah) is sunnah in it due to a hadith. Similarly, sitting on the minbar before beginning the khutbah during the adhan in front of him—this is a tradition. The adhan in front of him is like the iqamah which is sunnah after the khutbah for the prayer. Then he stands after the adhan for the two khutbahs; if he sits for both or for one, it suffices but is disliked without excuse. In al-Walwaji: If he delivers the khutbah lying down, it suffices. As in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. And when he stands, the sword is in his left hand, leaning on it. As in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. In al-Khulasa: It is disliked for him to deliver the khutbah leaning on a bow or staff. In al-Muhit: That is against the sunnah. In Rawdat al-Ulama: The wisdom behind the khatib delivering the khutbah leaning on a sword in every town conquered by force is to show them that it was conquered by the sword, and if you turn away from Islam, the Muslims will fight you with it until you return to Islam. And he delivers without it in a town conquered peacefully. The Prophet's city was conquered by the Quran, so the khatib delivers without a sword. Mecca was conquered by the sword, so he delivers with a sword. And it is sunnah to face the people with his face; if he delivers facing the qibla with his back to the people, it is disliked, as in al-Khulasa. The people should face him with their faces during the khutbah because he is admonishing and addressing them. Turning away from him is negligence and harshness. Shams al-A'imma said: Whoever is in front of the imam should face him; whoever is to the right or left should turn towards the imam. It is established that the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, used to face his companions when delivering the khutbah. Whoever was in front of him faced him; whoever was to his right or left turned towards him. Imam al-Sarakhsi said: The custom in our time is to face...
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage lists the sunan (recommended acts) of the Friday khutbah. The number is approximately eighteen, with room for addition. The sunan are divided into those pertaining to the khatib (preacher) and those pertaining to the khutbah itself. Key sunan include: the khatib sitting in his place to the right of the minbar before the khutbah, wearing black (though white is also recommended), purification (taharah) during the khutbah—though it is not a condition, as the khutbah is dhikr and does not require the same conditions as prayer; covering the private parts; sitting on the minbar during the adhan; standing for the khutbah; holding a sword in the left hand if the city was conquered by force (to remind of the power of Islam), and not holding a sword if conquered peacefully; facing the people, and the people facing the khatib. The passage notes that the khutbah is not like prayer in requiring face toward the qibla or prohibition of speech. It also addresses a misunderstanding: the hadith that the khutbah is 'half of prayer' refers to reward, not to legal conditions. There is a narration from Abu Yusuf that taharah is a condition, but the relied-upon view is that it is sunnah. The text ends with a citation from al-Sarakhsi about the custom of facing.

Key Terms
سنة (sunnah): A recommended act that is not obligatory; omitting it is not sinful but disliked.
طهارة (taharah): Ritual purity from major and minor impurity; not a condition for the khutbah, but sunnah.
سيف (sayf): Sword; the khatib leans on it during the khutbah in cities conquered by force, as a symbol.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Regarding taharah (purity) for the Friday khutbah, which statement is correct according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) It is a condition; without it the khutbah is invalid.
B) It is sunnah; the khutbah is valid without it, but disliked.
C) It is wajib, and omitting it requires repeating the khutbah.
D) It is only required for the khatib, not for the congregation.

Key Takeaway
The sunan of the khutbah are commendable, but the khutbah remains valid even if some are omitted; the only condition for validity is the correct intention of the khatib to give the Friday sermon.

Sunnah Acts of the Friday Khutbah
Enumerating sunan of the Friday khutbah with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the sunan of the Friday khutbah and explain why the Hanafi school considers them sunnah muakkadah rather than wajib.

Core Idea
The sunan of the Friday khutbah are emphasized but not obligatory; omission is makruh, not invalid.

This lesson unpacks the recommended acts (sunan) of the Friday khutbah as presented in Imdad al-Fattah. You will learn what these acts are, the evidence for them, and why the Hanafi school treats them as sunnah muakkadah rather than as obligations. Understanding this distinction is crucial to appreciating the khutbah's flexibility while preserving its prophetic model.

Guided Path
1
List the sunan of the khutbah
Identify each sunnah mentioned in the passage: beginning with hamd, extolling Allah, shahadatan, salawat, admonition, reminding, reciting a Quranic verse, two khutbahs, sitting between them, repeating hamd and salawat in the second, supplication, and listening.
The passage provides detailed descriptions of each.
2
Examine the evidence for reciting a verse
The author cites tawatur reports that the Prophet (peace be upon him) recited Quranic verses in his khutbah. This is the primary proof that reciting a verse is sunnah.
Tawatur establishes the act itself but not its legal classification.
3
Analyse why two khutbahs are sunnah not wajib
The free text poses the question: why are two khutbahs not obligatory like Surah al-Fatihah in prayer? The answer: the sunnah evidence is zanni al-dalala because it is contradicted by the report of 'Uthman, and obligation requires qat'i evidence.
This illustrates the Hanafi usul principle that zanni dalala cannot create wujub.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi differences
Note subtle differences: Abu Yusuf emphasizes ta'awwudh, Ibn Abi Layla has a different view on the sitting, and the measure of sitting (three verses) is from zahir al-riwayah.
These differences do not affect the overall mufta bihi.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a khatib who omits the ta'awwudh and recites only a short verse without basmalah. According to the passage, the khutbah is valid but makruh tanzihi; the acts are sunnah muakkadah.
This demonstrates the practical consequence of the sunnah muakkadah classification.

والسلام ) قرأ في خطبته: ﴿وَاتَّقُوا يَوْمًا تُرْجَعُونَ فِيهِ إِلَى اللَّهُ [البقرة : ۲۸۱]»، وروي أنه قرأ : تَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا اتَّقُوا اللَّهَ وَقُولُوا قَوْلًا سَدِيدًا) [الاختران (۷۰)، وروي أنه قرأ: ﴿وَنَادَوْا يملك لِيَقْضِ عَلَيْنَا رَبُّكَ قَالَ [الرو: ۷۷]، وروي أنه قرأ: ﴿إِذَا زُلْزِلَتِ الْأَرْضُ زِلْزَا لَهَا ) [ا: ١] وإذا قرأ سورة تامة يتعوذ ثم يسمي قبلها، وإن قرأ آية قال بعضهم: يتعوذ ثم يسمي، وأكثرهم قالوا: يتعوذ ولا يسمي، ولهذا تعارف الخطباء ترك التسمية أحياناً، والإتيان بالتعوذ علـى كــل حــال يقولون: أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم، وأصل الاختلاف في القراءة في غير الخطبة إذا أراد أن يقرأ سورة يتعوذ ثم يسمي، وإذا أراد أن يقرأ آية هل يسمي؟ فيه اختلاف (وَ) سُنَّ (خُطبتان) للتوارث إلى وقتنا، فإن قيل: لم لا يجب خطبتان بالسنة كما وجبت الفاتحة بالسنة؟؟ قلنا: إن السنة غير قطعية الدلالة لتعـارضـهـا بخـبـر عثمان، فلا يثبـت بـهـا الوجوب، كما في «السراج»، (وَ) سن (الجلوس بــيـن الخطبتين) قال في ( المحيط ) : يجلس جلسة خفيفة بينهما ) ، وقال شمس الأئمة « السرخسي» : إذا تمكن في موضع جلوسه، واستقر كل عضو منه في موضعه، قام من غير مكث ولبث، وكان ابن أبي ليلى يقول: إذا مس الأرض موضع جلوسه أدنى مئة قام إلى الخطبة الأخرى، وفي ( السغناقي) ظاهر الرواية مقدار ثلاث آيات ومثله في «التجنيس»، (وَ) سن (إعادةُ الحَمْدِ و) إعادة الثناء، والصَّلاةِ على سيدنا النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في ابتداء الخطبة الثانية والدعاء فيها للمؤمنين والمؤمنات بالاستغفار لهم، وأن يَسْمَعَ القومُ الخُطبة. وتخفيفُ الخطبتين بقَدْرِ سُورة من طِوَالِ المُفَصَّل، ويُكْرَهُ التطويلُ وتَرْكُ شيءٍ مِنَ السُّنَنِ. ويجبُ السَّعْيُ..
And its beginning with praise of Allah, and extolling Him as is befitting of Him. And the two testimonies, and sending blessings upon our Master the Prophet. And admonition. And reminding. And recitation of a verse from the Quran. And two sermons and sitting between the two sermons, and repeating the praise and. The people facing the qiblah and leaving the khatib not facing them due to the difficulty of straightening the rows after the khatib finishes his sermon because of the crowding; he said: this is best, as in al-Tatar Khaniyya from al-Muhit. (And) it is sunnah (his beginning with praise of Allah) after seeking refuge in himself secretly, and it is sunnah, as in Sharh al-Maqdisi, and in al-Bahr from al-Qunyah, Abu Yusuf said: It is befitting for the khatib, when he ascends the minbar, to seek refuge with Allah in himself before the sermon. (And extolling Him, exalted is He, as is befitting of Him, and the two testimonies, and sending blessings upon our Master the Prophet, peace be upon him, and admonition) by deterring from sins, intimidation, and warning of what necessitates Allah's wrath and punishment, (and reminding) by what leads to salvation and success in this world and the next, (and recitation of a verse from the Quran). He said in al-Muhit: He recites in the sermon a surah from the Quran or a verse; for reports have reached tawatur that the Prophet, peace be upon him, used to recite the Quran in his sermon, not devoid of a surah or a verse from the Quran. It is narrated that he, peace be upon him, recited in his sermon: {And fear a Day when you will be returned to Allah} [al-Baqarah 2:281], and it is narrated that he recited: {O you who have believed, fear Allah and speak words of appropriate justice} [al-Ahzab 33:70], and it is narrated that he recited: {And they will call, 'O Malik, let your Lord put an end to us!' He will say,} [al-Zukhruf 43:77], and it is narrated that he recited: {When the earth is shaken with its [final] earthquake} [al-Zalzalah 99:1]. And if he recites a complete surah, he seeks refuge then says the basmalah before it; but if he recites a verse, some said: he seeks refuge then says the basmalah, and most said: he seeks refuge and does not say the basmalah. For this reason, the khatibs customarily omit the basmalah sometimes, and always bring the ta'awwudh, saying: 'I seek refuge with Allah from the accursed Shaytan.' The origin of the dispute is about recitation outside the sermon: if he wants to recite a surah, he seeks refuge then says the basmalah; if he wants to recite a verse, should he say the basmalah? There is a dispute about it. (And) it is sunnah (two sermons) by continuous practice until our time. For if it is said: Why are two sermons not obligatory like the Fatihah is obligatory by sunnah? We say: The sunnah is not definitive in its indication because it is contradicted by the report of 'Uthman, so obligation is not established by it, as in al-Siraj. (And) it is sunnah (sitting between the two sermons). He said in al-Muhit: He sits a light sitting between them. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: When he is settled in his sitting place and every limb is established in its place, he stands without pause and delay. Ibn Abi Layla used to say: When his sitting place touches the ground ever so slightly, he stands for the other sermon. And in al-Sighnaqi, the zahir al-riwaya is the measure of three verses, and similar in al-Tajnis. (And) it is sunnah (repeating the praise and) repeating the extolling, and sending blessings upon our Master the Prophet, peace be upon him, at the beginning of the second sermon, and supplication in it for believing men and women by seeking forgiveness for them, and that the people listen to the sermon. And shortening the two sermons to the extent of a surah from the long mufassal, and prolonging is disliked, and leaving any of the sunan is disliked. And sa'y (walking) is obligatory.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary on Nur al-Idah, enumerates the sunan (recommended acts) of the Friday khutbah. The author opens by stating that the khutbah begins with praise of Allah, then lists the sunan: beginning with hamd (praise) after silently seeking refuge (ta'awwudh), extolling Allah, the two shahadahs, sending salawat upon the Prophet, admonition (wa'z), reminding (tadhkir), reciting a Quranic verse, delivering two khutbahs, sitting between them, and repeating the hamd, thana', and salawat in the second khutbah. For each sunnah, the commentary provides evidence or scholarly opinion. For instance, the recitation of a verse is supported by tawatur reports of the Prophet doing so; the two khutbahs are sunnah by continuous practice, and a question about why they are not obligatory is answered by noting that the sunnah evidence is not definitive (zanni al-dalala). The sitting between khutbahs is described as a light sitting. The second khutbah includes supplication for believers. The congregation is to listen attentively. The khutbah should be brief, about the length of a surah from the long mufassal (e.g., al-Hujurat to al-Buruj). Prolonging is makruh. Leaving any of these sunan is also makruh. Finally, the passage asserts that sa'y (walking to the Friday prayer) is obligatory (wajib), clarifying that it means walking calmly with dignity, not running, supported by the hadith 'When the prayer is established, do not come running but come walking with tranquility.' This aligns with the Hanafi position that sa'y in the verse (al-Jumu'ah 62:9) means going, not hastening.

Key Terms
الخطبة (al-khutbah): The Friday sermon delivered by the khatib before the prayer, consisting of two parts with a sitting between them.
السعي (as-sa'y): Walking to the Friday prayer, specifically with calmness and dignity, not running.
التعوذ (at-ta'awwudh): Saying 'A'udhu billahi min ash-shaytan ir-rajim' silently before beginning the khutbah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on leaving any of the sunan of the Friday khutbah?
A) It invalidates the khutbah.
B) It is disliked (makruh).
C) It is permissible without dislike.
D) It requires repeating the khutbah.

Key Takeaway
The sunan of the Friday khutbah are emphasized but not obligations; omission incurs dislike, not invalidity.

Sunnahs of the Second Friday Khutbah
Explains the sunnahs of the second Friday khutbah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah and mustahsan acts of the second Friday khutbah and explain their basis in established practice (tawaruth).

Core Idea
The second Friday khutbah has its own sunnahs: praise, blessings, and supplication – established by practice, not explicit text.

The Friday khutbah is divided into two parts, each with its own recommended elements. While the first khutbah focuses on admonition, the second khutbah has distinct sunnah acts that have been transmitted through the practice of the early community (tawaruth). This passage from Nur al-Idah and its commentary clarifies exactly what those acts are and their level of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main sunnah acts
The text lists three acts: praise (thana'), blessings on the Prophet (salat) at the beginning of the second khutbah, and supplication (du'a) for believing men and women. Note that these are sunnah, not wajib.
All three are based on tawaruth (established practice).
2
Distinguish between sunnah and mustahsan
The text uses 'sunnah' for the three acts and 'mustahsan' for mentioning the Rightly Guided Caliphs and the two uncles. Understand that mustahsan is a lower level of recommendation.
Mustahsan means 'considered good' but not as strongly emphasized as sunnah.
3
Understand the basis: tawaruth
The commentator explicitly states that these practices come from tawaruth – continuous practice of the early community. This is a key Hanafi methodology when no explicit hadith is available.
Tawaruth is a source of sunnah in the Hanafi school.
4
Identify the recommended wording
The text recommends a specific wording for the beginning of the second khutbah: 'Alhamdulillah nāḥmāduhu wa nastaʿīnuhu...' as it was the practice of the Prophet ﷺ.
This wording is a model, not obligatory.

Praise, and prayer upon our master the Prophet ﷺ at the beginning of the second khutbah, and supplication therein for believing men and women seeking forgiveness for them. (Praise, and) the repetition (of prayer upon our master the Prophet) (this repetition being) (at the beginning of the second khutbah) by tradition. It is recommended that the second khutbah be like this: 'Alhamdulillah nāḥmāduhu wa nastaʿīnuhu...' etc., because this is the second [khutbah] which the Messenger of Allah ﷺ used to deliver. (And mentioning the rightly-guided caliphs and the two uncles) is considered good (mustaḥsan); this is how it has been transmitted. Thus in al-Tajnīs wa al-Mazīd. (And) it is Sunnah to make supplication therein, i.e., in the second khutbah, (for believing men and women) in place of admonition as in al-Muḥīṭ and al-Ṭaḥāwī (with seeking forgiveness for them; the bā is in the meaning of 'with', meaning he supplicates for them for granting blessings, removal of afflictions, victory over enemies, and protection from illnesses and diseases along with seeking forgiveness.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage outlines the sunnahs of the second Friday khutbah. It is Sunnah to begin the second khutbah with praise (thana') of Allah and sending blessings (salat) upon the Prophet ﷺ, following the established tradition (tawaruth). The commentator recommends the specific wording 'Alhamdulillah nāḥmāduhu...' as practiced by the Prophet. Mentioning the Rightly Guided Caliphs and the Prophet's uncles (Hamza and Abbas) is considered mustahsan (recommended). Additionally, it is Sunnah to make supplication (du'a) for believing men and women, replacing the admonition of the first khutbah. The supplication includes asking for blessings, victory, and protection from illnesses, along with seeking forgiveness. These rulings are derived from the practice of the early community.

Key Terms
الثناء (ath-thana'): Praise of Allah, specifically the repetition of praise at the beginning of the second khutbah.
التوارث (at-tawaruth): Established practice transmitted from the early generations.
مستحسن (mustaḥsan): Considered good or recommended, but not at the level of sunnah muakkadah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The second Friday khutbah has its own recommended acts (sunnah and mustahsan) based on established practice, not explicit obligation.

Listening to the Khutbah and Shortening It
Rulings on listening and shortening the Friday khutbah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on listening to the Friday khutbah and its recommended length, and identify the evidence from the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud.

Core Idea
Listening to the khutbah is sunnah, not wajib; shortening it is recommended, prolongation is makruh.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah presents the Hanafi view on two key aspects of the Friday khutbah: the congregation's role in listening and the length of the sermon. While some schools of thought consider listening obligatory, the Hanafi school holds it as a sunnah act. The passage also provides a clear benchmark for the khutbah's length — the extent of a medium surah from the lengthy mufassal — and discourages prolonging, especially in short winter days.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
In the passage, locate the rulings on: (a) listening to the khutbah, (b) shortening the khutbah, and (c) the dislike of prolonging. Note the hukm level for each.
Listening is sunnah; shortening is sunnah; prolongation is makruh.
2
Examine the evidence
Find the hadith cited from Ibn Mas'ud: 'Lengthening the prayer and shortening the khutbah is from the understanding of a man.' Understand how this hadith supports the sunnah of brevity and the dislike of length.
The hadith is a sahih hadith (Muslim), but its dalalah is zanni.
3
Understand the reasoning
The commentator explains that even a brief remembrance qualifies as a khutbah if the place and time are prepared. This reinforces that a long khutbah is not required; brevity is praised.
4
Apply the ruling
Consider a real scenario: a Friday khutbah in winter that lasts 45 minutes. According to this passage, this is makruh tanzihan because it exceeds the recommended length of a mufassal surah. The imam should shorten it.
The passage also notes that speech may differ by location; the principle is to observe the benchmark.

(And) it is Sunnah that the people listen to the khutbah, and he raises his voice [in the second] but not in the first, as in al-TatarKhaniyya from 'al-Yanābīʿ', and if they do not hear it, it is acceptable as in Mirqāt al-Dirāya. (And) it is Sunnah to shorten both khutbahs, so the maximum length of the two khutbahs is the extent of a sūrah from the lengthy mufaṣṣal, and prolongation is disliked (makrūh) as in Mirqāt al-Dirāya without a time limit. In al-TatarKhaniyya from 'al-Ḥujja': it is disliked to prolong the khutbah in winter days because the days are short, so the long khutbah is not recommended. But he said before that: do not prolong the khutbah; Ibn Masʿūd said: 'Lengthening the prayer and shortening the khutbah is from the understanding of a man' (2) end. Succinct speech in such a situation is considered long because the place is prepared for the khutbah, the time is the time of khutbah, and the khaṭīb has prepared himself; so if he delivers a remembrance even if brief, it is a khutbah. It is not unlikely that speech differs according to the location, as in Mirqāt al-Dirāya. (It is) disliked (karāha) to leave any of the sunnahs we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage covers three rulings related to the Friday khutbah. First, it is Sunnah for the congregation to listen attentively; if some do not hear due to distance, the khutbah remains valid. Second, it is Sunnah to keep the khutbah concise, with the recommended maximum being the length of a medium surah from the lengthy mufassal (from Surah Qaf to Surah Buruj). Prolonging the khutbah is makruh tanzihan (disliked), especially in winter when days are short. The commentator cites the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud that brevity reflects religious understanding. Third, it is makruh to omit any of the established sunnahs of the khutbah. The explanation clarifies that even a brief speech qualifies as a khutbah if the formalities are observed.

Key Terms
طوال المفصل (tiwāl al-mufaṣṣal): The longer surahs of the mufassal section of the Quran, generally from Qaf to the end.
يكره (yukrahu): Disliked (makruh tanzihan), not sinful but better avoided.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Listening to the Friday khutbah is an emphasized sunnah, not an obligation; the khutbah should be concise, around the length of a medium surah from the mufassal.

Obligation of Walking and Prohibition of Sale on Jumu'ah
Rules for walking to Jumu'ah and the prohibition of selling after the first adhan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the obligation of walking to Jumu'ah and the prohibition of sale, and explain which adhan triggers these obligations.

Core Idea
The first adhan after zawal triggers the obligations of walking to Jumu'ah and abandoning sale.

The Quran commands believers to hasten to the remembrance of Allah and leave trade when called to the Friday prayer (Surah al-Jumu'ah 62:9). Hanafi jurists clarify what this call is: the first adhan after noon, introduced in the time of 'Uthman. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the intra-madhhab dispute about the trigger time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is ruled upon? The act of walking (sa'y) to Jumu'ah and the action of selling. The ruling for both is wajib (obligatory) – walking is obligatory, and leaving sale is obligatory.
Sa'y here means walking calmly, not running.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the Quranic verse (62:9) which commands 'fa-s'aw' (so hasten/walk) and 'dharu al-bay'a' (leave trade). This is a clear command, indicating obligation in Hanafi usul.
The verse is mutawatir in transmission (qat'i al-thubut) but its meaning is open to interpretation (zanni al-dalala).
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
In Hanafi usul, the imperative form (amr) indicates obligation (wujub) unless there is a contrary indicator. Both 'fa-s'aw' and 'dharu' are imperatives, so both are wajib.
Contrast with other schools that may interpret 'sa'y' as recommended.
4
Analyze the dispute about the trigger adhan
Which adhan triggers the obligation? Two views: the first adhan after zawal (added by 'Uthman) or the second adhan at the minbar. The preferred Hanafi view is the first adhan, as it provides timely notification for distant attendees.
Al-Tahawi preferred the second adhan, but the majority (including al-Hidayah) take the first.
5
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the trigger is the first adhan, then from that moment walking becomes wajib and selling becomes haram. If it is the second adhan, these obligations begin later.
The mufta bihi is the first adhan view.

Source Text

ويجبُ السَّعْيُ.. Commentary
This passage discusses the obligation of walking (sa'y) to Jumu'ah with dignity, and the prohibition of buying and selling after the first adhan. The author explains that 'sa'y' here means walking calmly, not running. The prohibition of sale is based on the Quranic command 'and leave off trade'. The ruling depends on which adhan triggers the obligation: the first adhan (after zawal, added by 'Uthman) or the second adhan (at the minbar). The preferred view (as in al-Hidayah) is that the first adhan after zawal triggers the obligation, because it serves as a timely notification for those far from the mosque. The passage also notes a difference of opinion: some like al-Tahawi consider the minbar adhan as the standard, but the majority of Hanafi scholars follow the first adhan view. The historical context of 'Uthman adding the first adhan is provided.

Key Terms
السعي (as-sa'y): Walking to the Friday prayer with dignity and calmness, not running.
الأذان الأول (al-adhān al-awwal): The first adhan for Jumu'ah, which is performed after zawal before the imam emerges.

Prayer and Speech When Imam Emerges: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on prayer and speech after imam emerges

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding prayer and speech after the imam emerges and identify the mufta bihi position with its evidence.

Core Idea
When the imam emerges, both nafl prayer and speech are prohibited — the text is explicit and general.

This lesson explores an important intra-Hanafi dispute about proper conduct during Jumu'ah. The key question: what is permissible once the imam steps out for the khutbah? We will examine the positions of the three leading Hanafi imams and understand the evidence that settles the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa: both prayer and speech are prohibited once the imam emerges, based on a prophetic hadith.
The hadith is explicit: 'When the imam emerges, there is no prayer and no speech.'
2
Identify the Sahibayn's view
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow speech before the khutbah begins, but differ on sitting silently.
Abu Yusuf permits sitting silently; Muhammad does not.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's dalil
Understand their reasoning: the prohibition is linked to the obligation of listening, which is not yet present before the khutbah.
They contrast speech with prayer because prayer extends and may interfere later.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's dalil
Abu Hanifa argues the hadith is general without differentiation, and speech may extend into the khutbah.
Thus both are prohibited from the moment the imam appears.
5
Determine thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference: Abu Hanifa forbids both; Sahibayn allow speech before khutbah. The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's due to stronger dalil and precaution.
Also note that only nafl prayer is prohibited; missed fard prayers are allowed even during khutbah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام الأعظم أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: (وإذا خرج الإمام فلا صلاة ولا كلام؛ لأن هذا نص النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : لا بأس بالكلام إذا خرج قبل أن يخطب، وإذا نزل قبل أن يكبر، واختلفا في جلوسه إذا سکت، فعند أبي يوسف يباح له، وعند محمد لا يباح له لهما أنَّ الكراهة للإخلال بفرض الاستماع ولا استماع هنا، بخلاف الصلاة لأنها تمتد، ولأبي حنيفة قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « إِذَا خَرَجَ الإمامُ فَلا صَلاةَ ولا كَلامَ ) من غير فصل، ولأن الكلام قد يمتد ، فأشبه الصلاة، كذا في « التبيين»، والمراد المنع من صلاة النافلة، وأما الفائتة فتجوز وقت الخطبة [ من غير كراهة، كما في « النهاية».
And Imam al-Aʿẓam Abu Ḥanīfa said: 'When the imam emerges, there is no prayer and no speech, because this is the text of the Prophet.' Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: There is no harm in speaking when he emerges before he delivers the khutbah, and when he descends before he says the takbir. And they differed regarding his sitting when he is silent: according to Abu Yusuf, it is permitted for him; according to Muhammad, it is not permitted. For them (the two), the dislike is due to violating the obligation of listening, and there is no listening here, unlike prayer because it extends. For Abu Hanifa, there is the Prophet's saying: 'When the imam emerges, there is no prayer and no speech' without differentiation, and because speech may extend, so it resembles prayer. Thus in al-Tabyīn. The intent is prohibition of supererogatory prayer; as for missed prayer, it is permitted during the time of the khutbah without dislike, as in al-Nihāyah.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of prayer and speech once the imam emerges for the Friday khutbah. Abu Hanifa (based on a hadith) holds that both voluntary prayer and speech are prohibited when the imam comes out, regardless of whether he has started the khutbah. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) allow speech before the khutbah begins, and they differ on whether sitting silently is permitted: Abu Yusuf allows it, Muhammad does not. Their reasoning is that the prohibition is tied to the obligation of listening, which is not yet present. Abu Hanifa argues that the hadith is general and that speech may extend into the khutbah. The commentator clarifies that the prohibition applies only to supererogatory (nafl) prayers, not to missed (fā'itah) prayers, which can be performed without dislike even during the khutbah.

Key Terms
النص (an-naṣṣ): The explicit text of the hadith that Abu Hanifa relies upon.
الفائتة (al-fā'itah): A missed obligatory prayer that is being made up.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permitted
B) Disliked but permissible
C) Prohibited
D) Recommended

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position prohibits both nafl prayer and voluntary speech once the imam emerges for Jumu'ah, based on the explicit and general hadith.

The Trigger for Jumu'ah Obligation: The First Adhan
States the obligation and trigger for Jumu'ah with an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when the obligation to leave business for Jumu'ah begins and distinguish the relied-upon position from the minority view.

Core Idea
The obligation to leave business for Jumu'ah is triggered by the first adhan after zawal, according to the most correct Hanafi view.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the obligation of attending Jumu'ah and the prohibition of business. It addresses an important intra-Hanafi dispute: which adhan triggers this obligation? We will unpack the evidence and see why the first adhan after zawal is the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its source
The text states that it is obligatory (fard) to go to Jumu'ah and leave business. This is based on the Quranic imperative 'wa dharu al-bay' (And leave business) from Surah al-Jumu'ah 62:9, which in Hanafi usul indicates obligation.
2
Determine the trigger for the obligation
The passage specifies that the obligation is triggered by 'the first adhan' and clarifies that this is 'after zawal' (the sun's zenith). The phrase 'fi al-ashah' indicates this is the most correct view.
First adhan refers to the adhan introduced in 'Uthman's time, not the adhan at the minbar.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Tahawi held that the trigger is the adhan at the minbar after the imam emerges, following the original practice of the Prophet. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi preferred the first adhan because it provides earlier notification and prevents missing the khutbah.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Understand the tarjih (preference)
The text declares the first adhan as the most correct (al-ashah) and cites authorities like al-Hidayah and al-'Inaya. The reasoning: waiting for the second adhan risks missing the sunnah, the khutbah, or even the prayer itself due to distance.
The tarjih is based on practical benefit and the historical acceptance of 'Uthman's addition.
5
Note the historical context and ijma
The first adhan was added by 'Uthman when the Muslim population grew. It was called from a house called al-Zawra' in the market, and no Companion objected, establishing a tacit ijma.
This strengthens the authority of the first adhan as the trigger.

For Friday (prayer) and leaving business, with the first adhan according to the most correct (view). And when the imam comes out, there is no prayer and no speech. He said: 'And what you have missed, complete it' (1) end. There is no harm in riding for Friday and the two Eids, but walking is better for the one who is able, and in returning from prayer, some said it is like going and some said it is like going out for other needs, and this is the most correct. Commentary
This passage begins by stating the ruling on attending Jumu'ah and leaving business. It is obligatory (fard) to go to Jumu'ah and to cease all business activities that may distract from it. The trigger for this obligation is the first adhan after the sun's zenith (zawal), according to the most correct view. The text then discusses riding versus walking: while riding is permissible, walking is superior for those able. The preferred opinion is that returning from prayer is like going to other needs, not like the initial going. The obligation to leave business extends to any activity that prevents one from going. The text addresses the adhan issue: some, like al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, argue the first adhan suffices for notification, while al-Tahawi held that the adhan at the minbar after the imam emerges is the original practice from the Prophet's time. The relied-upon position (al-ashah) is that the first adhan after zawal triggers the obligation, as stated in al-Hidaya. The historical note about 'Uthman adding the first adhan on a house called al-Zawra' confirms its acceptance by the Companions. The passage clarifies that the second adhan (at the minbar) is not the criterion for the prohibition of business.

Key Terms
الأذان الأول (al-adhan al-awwal): The first call to prayer for Jumu'ah, introduced in the time of 'Uthman after the sun's zenith, to notify people before the imam emerges.
الأذان عند المنبر (al-adhan 'inda al-minbar): The second call to prayer that occurs when the imam comes out and sits on the minbar.

Prohibition of Prayer and Speech When the Imam Emerges for Jumu'ah
Explains prohibition and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prayer and speech when the imam emerges for Jumu'ah, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When the imam emerges for Jumu'ah, all nafl prayer and speech are prohibited — Abu Hanifa's absolute ruling prevails.

The moment the imam steps out for the Friday sermon, a new set of rules takes effect. This lesson examines the primary prohibition of prayer and speech, the intra-Hanafi disagreement on its scope, and why the relied-upon position is absolute. Understanding this will help you observe the proper adab of Jumu'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is prohibited when the imam emerges? According to Abu Hanifa, both prayer and speech are haram. But which prayer? Only nafl (supererogatory). Missed fard prayers are allowed during the khutbah.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'When the imam comes out, there is no prayer and no speech.' This is a general, unqualified statement. In Hanafi usul, the negation with 'la' over a generic noun indicates absolute prohibition (nahy).
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalalah) according to Abu Hanifa.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The literal wording prohibits both acts without exception. Abu Hanifa takes it at face value. The Sahibayn, however, restrict it: they argue speech is only prohibited when it interferes with listening, which is not yet required before the khutbah begins.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) allow speech before the khutbah and during the imam's silence between ascent and khutbah. Abu Yusuf permits speech during the entire silence; Muhammad does not. They also differ on Tahiyyat al-masjid during khutbah.
The difference stems from the underlying illa: speech that distracts vs. absolute textual prohibition.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's absolute prohibition. This is stated in al-Hidayah and supported by al-Tabyin and al-Nihayah. The mufta bihi is that both nafl prayer and all speech are haram from the moment the imam appears.
This applies until the Jumu'ah prayer concludes, though speech during the khutbah itself is even more clearly prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام الأعظم أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: (وإذا خرج الإمام فلا صلاة ولا كلام؛ لأن هذا نص النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : لا بأس بالكلام إذا خرج قبل أن يخطب، وإذا نزل قبل أن يكبر، واختلفا في جلوسه إذا سکت، فعند أبي يوسف يباح له، وعند محمد لا يباح له لهما أنَّ الكراهة للإخلال بفرض الاستماع ولا استماع هنا، بخلاف الصلاة لأنها تمتد، ولأبي حنيفة قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « إِذَا خَرَجَ الإمامُ فَلا صَلاةَ ولا كَلامَ ) من غير فصل، ولأن الكلام قد يمتد ، فأشبه الصلاة، كذا في « التبيين»، والمراد المنع من صلاة النافلة، وأما الفائتة فتجوز وقت الخطبة من غير كراهة، كما في « النهاية».

لقصة عمر مع .
و
رواها مسلم والبخاري من حديث أبي هريرة له : « بَيْنَا عُمَرَ يَخْطُبُ إِذْ دَخَلَ عُثْمَانُ بْنُ عَفَّانَ له فَنَادَاهُ عُمَرُ: أية ساعة هذه فقال: إنِّي شُغِلْتُ اليومَ فَلَمْ أنْقَلِبُ إلَى أهْلِي حَتَّى سَمِعْتُ التَّاذِينَ، فَلَمْ أَزِدْ عَلَى أَنْ تَوَضَّأَتُ، فَقَالَ: وَالوُضُوءُ أَيْضَاً؟ وَقَدْ عَلِمْتُ أَنَّ النَّبِي صلى الله عليه وسلم كَانَ يَأْمُرُ بِالغُسْلِ » (3)، انتهى، كذا بخطه رحمه الله. ومن العلماء من قال: السكوت على القوم كان لازماً في زمن النبي ، لأنـه كـان يعرض عليهم في خطبته ما ينزل عليه من القرآن، فكان يلزمهم السكوت والاستماع ليأخذوا ويقبلوا منه، فأما في زماننا فالسكوت غير لازم، لأنه قد يكون في القوم من هو أعلم من الإمام وأورع، فلا يؤمر باستماع وعظ من هو دونه ومنهم من قال ما دام في حمد الله تعالى والثناء عليه والوعظ فعليهم الاستماع، وإذا أخذ في مدح ا الظلمة والدعاء فلا بأس بالكلام، وكان الطحاوي رحمه الله يقول: عـلـى القوم أن يستمعوا إلى مبلغ الخطيب قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ وَمَلَيكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا صَلُّوا عَلَيْهِ وَسَلِّمُوا تَسْلِيمًا [الأجْرَاب : ٥٦]، فحينئذ يجب عليهم أن يصلوا على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وفي « الـجـامـع الحسامي : يصلي السامع في نفسه ويخفي، ومثله في « قاضي خان»، وفي « الأوزجندي»: الأصــح السكوت إذا قال الخطيب: يايها الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا صَلُّوا عَلَيْهِ [ الاخْتِرَاب : ٥٦] وفي ( الحجة : ولــو سـكـت فهو أفضل تحقيقاً للإنصات، وفي ( المحيط » : والذي عليه عامة مشايخنا رحمهم الله أن عـلـى الـقــوم استماع الخطبة من أولها إلى آخرها، وفي فتح القدير عن أبي يوسف: ينبغي أن يصلي في نفسه
And Imam al-A'zam Abu Hanifa said: (When the imam comes out, there is no prayer and no speech; because this is the text of the Prophet. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: There is no harm in speaking when he comes out before he delivers the khutbah, and when he descends before he says the takbir, and they differed regarding his sitting when he remains silent: according to Abu Yusuf, it is permitted; according to Muhammad, it is not permitted. Their argument is that the prohibition is for violating the obligation of listening, and there is no listening here, unlike prayer because it extends. And for Abu Hanifa, the Prophet's statement: 'When the imam comes out, there is no prayer and no speech' without exception, and because speech may extend, so it resembles prayer. Thus in al-Tabyin. And what is meant is the prohibition of supererogatory prayer, but the missed (fard) prayer is permissible during the time of the khutbah without dislike, as in al-Nihaya. And his statement: 'When one of you comes and the preacher is delivering the khutbah, let him pray two rak'ahs' (3) narrated by Muslim is interpreted as before the prohibition of speech, i.e., prayer. As in the commentary of al-Maqdisi. And speech in general is prohibited. In al-Muhit: It is prohibited for the congregation to speak. In al-Hujja: Even if it is little with what resembles people's speech, and what resembles commanding good other than the imam. As for the imam's commanding good and forbidding evil, it is an obligatory sermon that does not break the khutbah in meaning. And the obligation upon the people is to listen and be silent, and speech prevents that, any speech. Similarly in al-Bada'i. In Fath al-Qadir: It is disliked for the preacher to speak during the khutbah due to disturbing the order, unless it is commanding good. 'Uthman, and it is well known. End. Shaykh of our shaykhs al-Maqdisi said: The story of 'Umar with . And it is narrated by Muslim and al-Bukhari from the hadith of Abu Hurayra: 'While 'Umar was delivering the khutbah, 'Uthman ibn 'Affan entered, so 'Umar called him: What hour is this? He said: I was occupied today and did not return to my family until I heard the call (to prayer), so I did no more than perform wudu'. He said: And wudu as well? And I know that the Prophet used to order ghusl.' End. Thus in his handwriting. Some scholars said: Silence was obligatory upon the people in the time of the Prophet because he used to present to them in his khutbah what was revealed to him of the Quran, so they were required to be silent and listen to take and accept from him. As for our time, silence is not necessary, because among the people there may be those who are more knowledgeable and more pious than the imam, so they are not commanded to listen to a sermon of someone inferior. And some said: As long as he is praising Allah and extolling Him and admonishing, they must listen; when he begins praising oppressors and supplicating, then there is no harm in speaking. Al-Tahawi used to say: The people must listen to the extent of the preacher's saying: 'Indeed Allah and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet. O you who believe, send blessings upon him and greet him with greetings of peace' [al-Ahzab: 56]. Then they must send blessings upon the Prophet. In al-Jami' al-Husami: The listener prays in himself and conceals it. Likewise in Qadi Khan. In al-Uzjandi: The most correct is silence when the preacher says: 'O you who believe, send blessings upon him' [al-Ahzab: 56]. In al-Hujja: Even if he remains silent, it is better for achieving attentiveness. In al-Muhit: And what the majority of our scholars agree upon is that the people must listen to the khutbah from beginning to end. In Fath al-Qadir from Abu Yusuf: He should pray in himself.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage addresses three interrelated rulings: (1) When the imam emerges for Jumu'ah, both prayer and speech are prohibited according to Imam Abu Hanifa, based on the direct hadith. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) allow speech before the khutbah begins, but differ on speech during the imam's silence. The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's, with the prohibition applying to nafl prayers; missed fard prayers are allowed during the khutbah. (2) The hadith about praying two rak'ahs upon entering while the preacher is delivering the khutbah (Tahiyyat al-masjid) is interpreted as being abrogated or applicable before the prohibition of speech. (3) During the khutbah, the congregation must listen attentively; speaking is generally prohibited. There are exceptions: the imam may speak for commanding good, and some scholars allow speech if the imam is praising oppressors. The story of 'Umar and 'Uthman illustrates the importance of ghusl for Jumu'ah. The passage concludes with various opinions on listening to the khutbah and the recommendation to send blessings on the Prophet silently.

Key Terms
خروج الإمام (khuruj al-imam): The moment the imam emerges from his place to ascend the pulpit for Jumu'ah.
صلاة النافلة (salat al-nafila): Supererogatory (voluntary) prayers, as opposed to obligatory (fard) prayers.
الإنصات (al-insat): Silent attentiveness; listening to the khutbah without speaking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is prohibited when the imam comes out for Jumu'ah?
A) Only speech
B) Only prayer
C) Both prayer and speech
D) Neither prayer nor speech

Key Takeaway
When the imam emerges for Jumu'ah, all nafl prayer and all speech are haram according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, based on the absolute hadith prohibition.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
6 min
Rulings on Speech, Silence, and Gestures During the Friday Khutbah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on speech and actions during the Friday sermon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on speech during the Friday khutbah, distinguish the exceptions, and identify the mufta bihi position for distant listeners and gestures.

Core Idea
Speech during the Friday sermon is haram for listeners; only necessary gestures and silent salawat are permitted.

The passage from Imdad al-Fattah presents the Hanafi position on what the congregation may or may not do during the Friday sermon. While the general rule is complete silence, there are nuanced exceptions for sending blessings, gesturing to correct wrong, and different considerations for those who cannot hear. This lesson walks through the evidence and the intra-Hanafi differences to clarify the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its evidence
From the passage, determine the primary ruling on speech during the khutbah for those who can hear. Note the sources cited: al-Muhit, al-Hujjah, al-Bada'i'. Also note the hadith of Umar and Uthman as evidence.
The ruling is that speech is haram because it negates the obligation of listening (istima').
2
Analyze the exceptions
Identify the exceptions: silent salawat when the verse of salawat is recited, and gestures (e.g., nodding) to command good. Note the difference of opinion on whether these are allowed.
Abu Yusuf allows silent salawat; gestures are correct according to al-Fath.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi disputes
Note the disagreements: some scholars said silence was only obligatory in the Prophet's time; others distinguish between parts of the sermon. Understand the Hanafi response that maintains the obligation of silence.
The relied-upon view upholds silence throughout the entire sermon.
4
Apply the rulings to the distant listener
For the person far away (na'i) who cannot hear the sermon, note the varying opinions: some allow recitation, others require silence. Identify the chosen view (al-mukhtar) that he should remain silent.
Al-Kamal prefers silence to avoid disturbing others.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi position
Consolidate the operative rulings: complete silence for listeners; silent salawat when verse is recited; gestures are permissible; distant listeners should remain silent.
This is the position from al-Muhit, Fath al-Qadir, and al-Walwaljiyah.

للفضيلتين وهـو الـصـواب، ويحمد في نفسه إذا عطس على الصحيح انتهى. وفي ( الينابيع : يكره التسبيح وقراءة القرآن، والصلاة على النبي والكتابة إذا كان يسمع الخطبة، انتهى، أي: إلا إذا تلا الخطيب قوله تعالى: إِنَّ اللَّهَ وَمَلَكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا صَلُّوا عَلَيْهِ وَسَلِّمُوا تَسْلِيمًا) فيصلي سراً كما ذكرناه. تنبيه لمن كان بعيداً: في ( المحيط» أما من كان
