كان يحرك شفتيه ويقرأ القرآن، وروى حماد عن إبراهيم رحمه الله أنه قال: إني أقرأ حزبين يوم الجمعة، والإمام يخطب، وفي ( الخانية ) : ويكلم الناس في التسبيح والتهليل عند الخطبة، قال بعضهم: من كان بعيداً عن الإمام ولا يسمع الخطبة يجوز له التسبيح والتهليل، وأجمعوا على أن من لا يسمع الخطبة لا يتكلم بكلام الناس، أما قراءة القرآن والتسبيح والذكر والفقه، قال بعضهم: الاشتغال بقراءة القرآن وبذكر الله تعالى أفضل، وقال بعضهم: الإنصات أفضل، وفي ( الولوالجية ) : النأي عن الخطيب إذا كان بحيث لا يسمع الخطبة لا يقرأ القرآن بل يسكت هو المختار ، قال الكمال: لأنه قد يصل إلى أذن من يسمع فيشغله عن فهم ما سمع، أو عن السماع بخلاف النظر في الكتاب والكتابة انتهى. وفي المحيط » : فأما . دراسة الفقه والنظر في كتب الفقه وكتابته، فمن أصحابنا رحمهم الله مـن كـره ذلك، ومنهم من . قال: لا بأس به، وكذا روي عن أبي يوسف، وقال الحسن بن زياد: ما دخــل الـعـراق أحد أفقه من الحكم بن زهير وإنَّ الحكم كان يجلس مع أبي يوسف يوم الجمعة وينظر في كتابه، ويصحح بالقلم وقت الخطبة ) ، انتهى. قال شمس الأئمة ( الحلواني رحمه الله تعالى وهـا هنـا [ فصل آخر ]: اختلف المشايخ رحمهم الله أيضاً في أنه إذا لم يتكلم بلسانه ولكنه أشار برأسه أو بيده، أو بعينه إن رأى منكراً من إنسان، فأشار برأسه هل يكره ذلك أم لا؟. فمن أصحابنا رحمهم الله من كره ذلك، وسوَّى بين الإشارة والتكلم باللسان، والصحيح أنه لا بأس به، كذا في «الفتح»، وفي التجنيس» فإنه روي عن عبد الله : بن مسعود ود، أنه « سلم على رسول الله يوم الجمعة وهو يخطب
His saying: (When one of you comes and the preacher is delivering the sermon, let him pray two rak'ahs) narrated by Muslim, is interpreted as before the prohibition of speech, i.e., prayer as in Sharh al-Maqdisi. Speech is unrestricted. In al-Muhit: It is forbidden for the congregation to speak. In al-Hujjah: Even if it is little and resembles people's speech, or resembles commanding good for other than the imam. As for the imam commanding good and forbidding evil, it is obligatory preaching that does not interrupt the sermon meaning-wise. The obligation on the congregation is listening and silence; speech negates that, whatever speech it is. Similarly in al-Bada'i'. In Fath al-Qadir: It is disliked for the preacher to speak during the sermon due to disrupting the order, unless it is commanding good. The story of Umar and Uthman is well-known. Sheikh of our sheikhs al-Maqdisi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The story of Umar with Uthman, narrated by Muslim and Bukhari from Abu Hurairah: 'While Umar was delivering the sermon, Uthman ibn Affan entered, and Umar called to him: What hour is this? He said: I was occupied today and did not return to my family until I heard the call to prayer, so I did no more than perform wudu. Umar said: Only wudu? And you know that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to order ghusl.' End quote from his writing. Some scholars said: Silence was obligatory for the congregation in the time of the Prophet because he would present to them what was revealed from the Quran, so silence and listening were obligatory to take and accept from him. As for our time, silence is not obligatory because among the congregation there may be someone more knowledgeable and pious than the imam, so they are not commanded to listen to the preaching of one inferior. And some said: As long as he is praising Allah and extolling Him and preaching, they must listen; but if he begins praising oppressors or making supplication, then speech is permissible. Al-Tahawi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The congregation must listen to the extent that the preacher recites the verse: 'Indeed, Allah and His angels confer blessings upon the Prophet. O you who have believed, ask blessings upon him and greet him with peace' (al-Ahzab: 56). Then they must send blessings upon the Prophet. In al-Jami' al-Husami: The listener prays in himself silently, similarly in Qadi Khan. In al-Uzjandi: The most correct is silence when the preacher says: 'O you who have believed, pray upon him' (al-Ahzab: 56). In al-Hujjah: Even if he remains silent, it is better to achieve silence. In al-Muhit: The general practice of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) is that the congregation must listen to the sermon from beginning to end. In Fath al-Qadir from Abu Yusuf: He should send blessings in himself because that does not distract him from hearing the sermon, thus achieving both virtues; that is correct. And he praises in himself if he sneezes, according to the correct view. In al-Yanabi': It is disliked to glorify Allah, recite Quran, send blessings, or write if he can hear the sermon. That is, except when the preacher recites the verse: 'Indeed, Allah and His angels confer blessings upon the Prophet...' then he prays silently as mentioned. A note for those far away: In al-Muhit: As for one who is far from the imam and does not hear what he says, there is no narration on this chapter. Muhammad ibn Salamah said he remains silent; this is narrated from Abu Yusuf. Al-Kamal said: That is more appropriate. Narrated from Nasr ibn Yahya: If he is far from the imam, he may recite Quran. Narrated that he would move his lips and recite Quran. Hammad narrated from Ibrahim (may Allah have mercy on him) that he said: I recite two hizbs on Friday while the imam delivers the sermon. In al-Khaniyah: People speak in glorification and tahleel during the sermon. Some said: One who is far from the imam and does not hear the sermon may engage in tasbih and tahleel. They all agreed that one who does not hear the sermon should not speak with people's speech. As for reciting Quran, tasbih, dhikr, and fiqh, some said: Occupying oneself with Quran and dhikr is better; others said: Silence is better. In al-Walwaljiyah: The one far from the preacher, if he does not hear the sermon, should not recite Quran but remain silent; that is the chosen view. Al-Kamal said: Because it might reach the ear of one who hears and distract him from understanding what he heard or from hearing, unlike looking at a book or writing. In al-Muhit: As for studying fiqh, looking in fiqh books, and writing, some of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) disliked that; others said it is permissible. Similarly narrated from Abu Yusuf. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad said: No one more learned in fiqh entered Iraq than al-Hakam ibn Zuhayr; al-Hakam used to sit with Abu Yusuf on Friday and look in his book and correct with a pen during the sermon. Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And here is another subsection: The scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) also differed regarding one who does not speak with his tongue but gestures with his head, hand, or eye if he sees reprehensible action from someone, gesturing with his head: is that disliked or not? Some of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) disliked it and equated gesture with speech; the correct view is that there is no harm, as in al-Fath. In al-Tajnis: It is narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud that he greeted the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) on Friday while he was delivering the sermon.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the rulings on speech, silence, and various actions during the Friday khutbah. It begins by citing a hadith about praying two rak'ahs when entering the mosque during the sermon, noting that this was before the prohibition of speech. The core ruling from al-Muhit is that it is forbidden (haram) for the congregation to speak during the khutbah, as their obligation is to listen attentively. Several authorities (al-Hujjah, al-Bada'i') affirm that any speech, even if little, invalidates the purpose of listening. However, exceptions are noted: the imam may speak to command good, and there is a story of Umar rebuking Uthman for not performing ghusl, implying that brief necessary speech is permissible. The passage then presents differences: some scholars hold that silence was only obligatory in the Prophet's era because revelation was being delivered; today, silence is not obligatory. Others distinguish between parts of the sermon: during praise and preaching, silence is required; during praise of oppressors or supplication, speech is allowed. Al-Tahawi and others say that when the verse of salawat is recited, the congregation must silently send blessings. Abu Yusuf recommends doing so because it does not distract from hearing. For those far away who cannot hear the sermon, there is disagreement: some say they must remain silent, others allow reciting Quran or dhikr, but they all agree that worldly speech is prohibited. The relied-upon view (al-mukhtar) is that the distant person should remain silent to avoid distracting others. Regarding studying fiqh or writing, there is a range of opinions from dislike to permissibility. Finally, a separate subsection discusses gesturing (e.g., with head or hand) during the sermon: some equate it with speech and thus dislike it, but the correct position (as in al-Fath) is that it is permissible, based on the practice of Ibn Mas'ud greeting the Prophet during the khutbah. Throughout, the passage shows the Hanafi method of citing multiple authorities and weighing evidence.

Key Terms
الخطبة (al-khutbah): The Friday sermon delivered by the imam before the prayer, consisting of praise, Quran recitation, and admonition.
الإنصات (al-insat): Silence and attentive listening to the sermon, considered obligatory for the congregation.
النأي (al-na'i): Being far from the imam such that one cannot hear the khutbah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a person who is far away from the imam and cannot hear the Friday khutbah, according to the chosen view (al-mukhtar) in this passage?
A) He may recite Quran aloud to benefit himself.
B) He should remain silent and not engage in any speech or recitation.
C) He may engage in tasbih and tahleel loudly.
D) He may study fiqh and write notes.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that silence is obligatory for those who can hear the Friday sermon, with limited exceptions for silent salawat and gestures; distant listeners should also remain silent.

Actions During Friday Khutba for Those Far from Imam
Rulings for those who cannot hear the Friday sermon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred action (mufta bihi) for a person who cannot hear the Friday khutba and explain why silence is preferred over other acts of worship.

Core Idea
For those far from the imam, silence is preferred to avoid distracting others.

When attending Friday prayer, some worshippers sit far from the khatib and cannot hear the sermon. What should they do? The Hanafi school has addressed this scenario with a careful balance between individual devotion and communal harmony. This lesson unpacks the rulings and the reasoning behind the preferred practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Categories
The text distinguishes between one who can hear the khutba and one who cannot. For each category, different rulings apply.
Hearing is the key differentiator.
2
Rule for Those Who Hear
For one who hears the khutba, tasbih, Quran recitation, sending blessings, and writing are makruh tanzih, except when the khatib recites the verse about blessings.
This is consistent with the obligation of insāt.
3
Acknowledge the Ikhtilaf for the Far
There is no explicit narration in the primary texts (Zahir al-Riwayah) for the far person. Some scholars permit Quran recitation, others prefer silence.
Muhammad ibn Salama and Abu Yusuf report silence; Nasr ibn Yahya and others permit recitation.
4
Apply the Tarjih
Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam and the author of Al-Walwaljiya choose silence, because even if the person is far, his recitation may reach someone who is listening and distract them.
This is a fatwa of later authorities (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
5
State the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position is that the far person should remain silent. Other acts like looking into fiqh books or writing are less likely to disturb and are permissible.
Worldly talk is forbidden for all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على النبي ؛ لأن ذلك مما لا يشغله عن سماع الخطبة، فكان إحرازاً للفضيلتين وهو الصواب، ويحمد في نفسه إذا عطس على الصحيح انتهى. وفي الينابيع: يكره التسبيح وقراءة القرآن، والصلاة على النبي والكتابة إذا كان يسمع الخطبة، انتهى، أي: إلا إذا تلا الخطيب قوله تعالى: إِنَّ اللَّهَ وَمَلَكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا صَلُّوا عَلَيْهِ وَسَلِّمُوا تَسْلِيمًا فيصلي سراً كما ذكرناه. تنبيه لمن كان بعيداً: في المحيط أما من كان بعيداً من الإمام لا يسمع ما يقول، فلا رواية في هذا الفصل، قال محمد بن سلمة يسكت، وروي هذا عن أبي يوسف قال الكمال: وهو أوجه انتهى. وروي عن نصر بن يحيى: إن كان بعيداً من الإمام يقرأ القرآن، وروي عنه أنه كان يحرك شفتيه ويقرأ القرآن، وروى حماد عن إبراهيم رحمه الله أنه قال: إني أقرأ حزبين يوم الجمعة، والإمام يخطب، وفي الخانية: ويكلم الناس في التسبيح والتهليل عند الخطبة، قال بعضهم: من كان بعيداً عن الإمام ولا يسمع الخطبة يجوز له التسبيح والتهليل، وأجمعوا على أن من لا يسمع الخطبة لا يتكلم بكلام الناس، أما قراءة القرآن والتسبيح والذكر والفقه، قال بعضهم: الاشتغال بقراءة القرآن وبذكر الله تعالى أفضل، وقال بعضهم: الإنصات أفضل، وفي الولوالجية: النأي عن الخطيب إذا كان بحيث لا يسمع الخطبة لا يقرأ القرآن بل يسكت هو المختار، قال الكمال: لأنه قد يصل إلى أذن من يسمع فيشغله عن فهم ما سمع، أو عن السماع بخلاف النظر في الكتاب والكتابة انتهى. وفي المحيط: فأما دراسة الفقه والنظر في كتب الفقه وكتابته، فمن أصحابنا رحمهم الله من كره ذلك، ومنهم من قال: لا بأس به، وكذا روي عن أبي يوسف، وقال الحسن بن زياد: ما دخل العراق أحد أفقه من الحكم بن زهير وإنَّ الحكم كان يجلس مع أبي يوسف يوم الجمعة وينظر في كتابه، ويصحح بالقلم وقت الخطبة، انتهى.
Upon the Prophet; because that does not occupy him from hearing the sermon, so it is an acquisition of both virtues and that is correct, and he praises in himself when he sneezes upon the correct view, end. And in Al-Yanabi': It is disliked to glorify, recite Quran, send blessings upon the Prophet, and write if one hears the sermon, end, meaning: except if the khatib recites the verse, 'Indeed Allah and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet, O you who believe, send blessings upon him and salute him with a salutation,' then he sends blessings silently as we mentioned. A note for one who is far: In Al-Muhit, as for one who is far from the imam and does not hear what he says, there is no narration on this chapter; Muhammad bin Salama said: he remains silent, and this is narrated from Abu Yusuf; al-Kamal said: and it is more appropriate, end. And it is narrated from Nasr bin Yahya: if he is far from the imam he recites Quran, and it is narrated from him that he would move his lips and recite Quran, and Hammad narrated from Ibrahim, may Allah have mercy on him, that he said: I recite two hizbs on Friday while the imam is delivering the sermon. And in Al-Khaniya: and people speak in glorification and tahil during the sermon. Some said: whoever is far from the imam and does not hear the sermon, it is permissible for him to glorify and say la ilaha illa Allah. And they agreed that whoever does not hear the sermon does not speak with the speech of people. As for recitation of Quran, glorification, dhikr, and fiqh, some said: occupying oneself with recitation of Quran and remembrance of Allah is better, and some said: listening attentively is better. And in Al-Walwaljiya: distance from the khatib when such that he does not hear the sermon, he does not recite Quran but remains silent; that is the chosen view. Al-Kamal said: because it may reach the ear of one who hears and distract him from understanding what he heard, or from hearing, unlike looking in the book and writing, end. And in Al-Muhit: as for studying fiqh, looking in fiqh books, and writing, some of our companions, may Allah have mercy on them, disliked that, and some of them said: it is fine, and similarly narrated from Abu Yusuf, and Al-Hasan bin Ziyad said: Iraq has not entered anyone more knowledgeable in fiqh than Al-Hakam bin Zuhayr, and Al-Hakam used to sit with Abu Yusuf on Friday and look in his book and correct with a pen during the sermon, end.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings regarding actions during the Friday khutba, particularly for those who are far from the imam and cannot hear the sermon. The text begins by affirming that it is permissible to send blessings upon the Prophet silently during the khutba, as it does not distract from listening, thus combining the virtues of sending blessings and listening. Sneezing and praising Allah (tashmit) is also done silently. The author then quotes from Al-Yanabi' that it is disliked to engage in tasbih, Quran recitation, sending blessings, or writing if one can hear the sermon, except when the khatib recites the verse about sending blessings, in which case one should send blessings silently. The core issue addressed is for those far away (al-ba'id): there is no explicit narration in the madhhab, but Muhammad bin Salama reported that such a person should remain silent, and this is considered more appropriate by al-Kamal. Other narrations permit Quran recitation, but the preferred view from Al-Walwaljiya is that such a person should remain silent, because his recitation might be heard by someone who is listening and distract them. However, looking at fiqh books and writing is not equivalent, as it is less likely to disturb others. Thus, the relied-upon position is that silence is best for those who cannot hear, to avoid any potential distraction.

Key Terms
الإنصات (al-insāt): Listening attentively to the khutba, which is the preferred action even for those far away.
النأي (al-na'y): Being far from the khatib such that one cannot hear the sermon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Send blessings upon the Prophet audibly
C) Remain silent
D) Engage in tasbih and tahleel

Key Takeaway
For a person who cannot hear the Friday khutba, remaining silent is the preferred action to avoid potential distraction to others.

Gesturing During Friday Khutba
Ruling on permissibility of gesturing during khutba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gesturing during Friday khutba, distinguish it from speaking, and cite the prophetic proof.

Core Idea
Gesturing during khutba is permissible; the Prophet’s own action proves it is not speech.

We already know that speaking during the Friday khutba is prohibited. But what about a simple gesture—nodding, pointing, or waving? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on non-verbal communication during the sermon, clarifying that gestures are permitted based on the Prophet's own practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage addresses gesturing (ishara) during khutba without speech—whether it is disliked (makruh) or permissible (mubah).
The controversy is within the Hanafi school itself.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars equated gestures to speech, considering them makruh. Others permitted them.
The author declares the correct view is permissibility.
3
Examine the prophetic proof
The hadith of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud: he greeted the Prophet during khutba. The Prophet did not reply verbally but gestured back. This shows that gesturing is not equivalent to speech.
The hadith also mentions the Prophet did not respond to a sneeze (tashmit), reinforcing prohibition of speech.
4
Derive the mufta bihi ruling
The correct (sahih) position is that gesturing is permissible (la ba'sa bihi). This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Sources: Al-Fath and Al-Tajnis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال شمس الأئمة الحلواني رحمه الله تعالى وها هنا فصل آخر: اختلف المشايخ رحمهم الله أيضاً في أنه إذا لم يتكلم بلسانه ولكنه أشار برأسه أو بيده، أو بعينه إن رأى منكراً من إنسان، فأشار برأسه هل يكره ذلك أم لا؟ فمن أصحابنا رحمهم الله من كره ذلك، وسوَّى بين الإشارة والتكلم باللسان، والصحيح أنه لا بأس به، كذا في الفتح، وفي التجنيس فإنه روي عن عبد الله بن مسعود أنه سلم على رسول الله يوم الجمعة وهو يخطب ولا يرد سلاماً، ولا يُشَمِّتُ عاطساً. [الظلمة؟] فرد عليه بالإشارة انتهى.
Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And here is another chapter: The mashayikh, may Allah have mercy on them, also differed concerning one who does not speak with his tongue but gestures with his head or hand, or with his eye if he sees an objectionable act from a person, so he gestures with his head: is that disliked or not? Some of our companions, may Allah have mercy on them, disliked that and equated gesturing with speaking with the tongue, and the correct view is that it is fine, as in Al-Fath. And in Al-Tajnis, it is narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud that he greeted the Messenger of Allah on Friday while he was delivering the sermon, and he (the Prophet) did not return the greeting verbally, nor did he respond to a sneeze. [الظلمة?] He replied to him by gesture, end.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage discusses whether gesturing (e.g., nodding, pointing) during the Friday khutba is disliked or permissible. Some Hanafi scholars disliked it, equating it to speaking. However, the correct (sahih) position is that it is fine. The proof is the hadith of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, who greeted the Prophet while he was delivering the khutba; the Prophet did not reply verbally but gestured in response. This indicates that gesturing is not equivalent to speech and is permissible when necessary, such as acknowledging a greeting or indicating something. The text cites Al-Fath and Al-Tajnis as sources supporting this view. Thus, a slight gesture without speech is allowed during the khutba.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-ishāra): Gesturing with the head, hand, or eye without speaking, as a means of communication during khutba.
التشميت (al-tashmīt): Responding to a sneeze by saying 'yarhamuka Allah' (may Allah have mercy on you).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The Prophet's response by gesture to Abdullah ibn Mas'ud's greeting

Key Takeaway
Gesturing during khutba is permitted because the Prophet himself gestured, proving it is not equivalent to speech.

Proximity to the Imam on Friday Is Preferred
Ruling on proximity to imam on Friday

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on proximity to the imam during Friday prayer and cite the supporting hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Sitting close to the imam on Friday is sunnah, supported by hadith rewarding early attendance and closeness.

This passage addresses whether it is better to sit close to or far from the imam during the Friday khutba and prayer. Some scholars preferred distance, but the correct Hanafi view, based on clear hadith, is that proximity is better. Understanding this ruling helps you practice a sunnah that brings great reward.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
We extract that the act is 'al-dunū min al-imām' (proximity to the imam) on Friday. The ruling is that it is better (afḍal) and sunnah.
The text explicitly says 'al-dunū minhu afḍal' and 'al-sunnah an yabkir wa yadnu'.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites two hadith: 'man bakara wabtakara...' and the hadith of angels recording attendees.
Both hadith are from standard Sunni collections (Abu Dawud, Bukhari, etc.).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly uses the word 'dana' (draw near) and promises great reward per step, indicating preference for proximity.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Many earlier scholars preferred distance to avoid hearing the imam's supplications, but the mashayikh of the Hanafi school chose proximity.
This ikhtilaf is resolved by the explicit hadith.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
It is sunnah to sit as close as possible, provided one does not harm others or step over people.
The text adds conditions: no harm, no stepping on clothes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال شمس الأئمة رحمه الله: وها هنا فصل آخر وهو الدنو من الإمام أهو أولى أو التباعد عنه؟ قال كثير من العلماء: التباعد أولى كيلا يسمع مدح ودعاؤهم، والصحيح من الجواب من مشايخنا رحمهم الله أن الدنو منه أفضل، وفي الدراية: والسنة أن يبكر ويدنو من الإمام ما أمكن من غير أن يؤذي أحداً، وبه قال الشافعي رحمه الله، لما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: من بكر وابتكر، ومشى ولم يركب، ودنا ولم يلغ، كتب له بكل خطوة عمل سنة، أجر قيامها وصيامها. وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: إذا كان يوم الجمعة وقفت الملائكة على باب المسجد فيكتبون الأول فالأول، فمثل المهجر أي: المبكر، كمثل المهدي بدنة، ثم الذي يليه كالمهدي بقرة، ثم الذي يليه كالمهدي شاة، ثم الذي يليه كالمهدي دجاجة، ثم الذي يليه كالمهدي بيضة، فإذا خرج الإمام طووا صحفهم وجلسوا يستمعون الذكر، كذا في الروضة، وجامع الكردي، ويستحب أن يجلس في الصف الأول، وتكلموا في الصف الأول قيل: هو خلف الإمام في المقصورة، وقيل: مما يلي المقصورة، وبه أخذ أبو الليث وفي خزانة الأكمل: هذا في حق العامة؛ لأنهم كانوا ممنوعين من دخول المقصورة، أما في زماننا فلا منع في الصف الأول الذي يلي الإمام، وإذا حضر والمسجد ملآن إن كان لا يؤذي الناس، ولا يطأ ثوباً لا بأس به، ويدنو من الإمام وإلا فلا يتخطى. ولا يرد سلاماً، ولا يُشَمِّتُ عاطساً كذا قال محمد في الأصل ولم يذكر فيه خلافاً، وروى محمد عن أبي يوسف في صلاة الأثر أنهم يردون السلام ويشمتون العاطس، فتبين أن ما في الأصل قول محمد رحمه الله تعالى، والخلاف بين أبي يوسف ومحمد في هذا بناء على أنه إذا لم يرد السلام في الحال هل يرده بعدما فرغ الإمام من الخطبة؟ على قول محمد يرد، وعلى قول أبي يوسف لا يرد، وروي عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله في غير رواية الأصول يرد بقلبه، ولا يرد بلسانه، ولم يذكر محمد في الأصل أن العاطس هل يحمد الله تعالى؟ ذكر الحسن بن زياد عن أبي حنيفة يحمد في نفسه ولا يجهر، وهذا صحيح، وعن محمد رحمه الله تعالى يحمد الله بقلبه ولا يحرك شفتيه، وهو الصحيح كما قدمناه، وفي النصاب: إذا شمت أو رد السلام في رحمه الله أنه
And Shams al-A'imma, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And here is another chapter, which is: proximity to the imam—is it better or distance from him? Many scholars said: distance is better so that one does not hear their praise and supplications, and the correct answer from our mashayikh, may Allah have mercy on them, is that proximity to him is better. And in Al-Diraya: It is sunnah to go early and draw near to the imam as much as possible without harming anyone, and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said likewise, based on what was narrated that he, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'Whoever goes early and comes early, and walks and does not ride, and draws near and does not engage in idle talk, for every step he takes he is rewarded with the deeds of a year, the reward of its standing and its fasting.' And he, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'On Friday, the angels stand at the door of the mosque and write down the first and then the next; the example of the early bird is like one who offers a camel as a sacrifice, then the one next to him like one who offers a cow, then the one next to him like one who offers a sheep, then the one next to him like one who offers a chicken, then the one next to him like one who offers an egg. When the imam comes out, they fold their scrolls and sit listening to the remembrance.' As in Al-Rawda and Jami' al-Kurdi. And it is recommended to sit in the first row, and they discussed the first row: it is said it is behind the imam in the maqsura, and it is said it is what is adjacent to the maqsura; Abu al-Layth adopted this. And in Khazanat al-Akmal: This is concerning the general public, because they were prevented from entering the maqsura; as for our time, there is no prevention in the first row that is next to the imam. And if one attends and the mosque is full, if he does not harm people and does not step on clothes, it is fine, and he draws near to the imam; otherwise he does not step over. And he does not return the greeting, nor does he respond to a sneeze. Thus said Muhammad in Al-Asl, and he did not mention any difference in it. And Muhammad narrated from Abu Yusuf in Salat al-Athar that they return greetings and respond to the sneezer. So it appears that what is in Al-Asl is the statement of Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, and the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in this is based on whether, if he does not return the greeting immediately, does he return it after the imam finishes the sermon? According to Muhammad, he returns; according to Abu Yusuf, he does not. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, in other than the principal narrations that he returns with his heart and does not return with his tongue. And Muhammad did not mention in Al-Asl whether the sneezer praises Allah? Al-Hasan bin Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa: he praises in himself and does not do it aloud, and this is correct. And from Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him: he praises Allah with his heart and does not move his lips, and that is correct as we have presented. And in Al-Nisab: if he responds to a sneeze or returns a greeting during...
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage covers two main issues. First, regarding whether it is better to sit close to the imam or far away during Friday prayer. Some scholars preferred distance to avoid hearing the imam's praise and supplications (which might lead to self-admiration), but the correct Hanafi view is that proximity is better, supported by hadith on the virtues of early attendance and closeness. The second issue is about responding to greetings (salam) and sneezes (tashmit) during the khutba. The base ruling from Muhammad al-Shaybani in Al-Asl is that one should not respond verbally. However, Abu Yusuf held that responding is permissible based on the practice of early Muslims recorded in his work Salat al-Athar. The dispute is analyzed: Muhammad says the response should be delayed until after the khutba, while Abu Yusuf says it should not be made even later. Abu Hanifa's position (outside the principal narrations) is to respond silently in the heart. For sneezing, the correct view is to praise Allah internally. The passage thus presents a detailed intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the permissibility of verbal responses during khutba.

Key Terms
الدنو من الإمام (al-dunū min al-imām): Coming close to the imam during the Friday khutba and prayer, which is considered better than staying far.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The reward of performing Hajj.
D) The reward of giving charity in gold.

Key Takeaway
Proximity to the imam on Friday is a sunnah that brings great reward, provided it does not cause harm to others.

Responding to Salam and Sneezes During the Friday Khutba: Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on responding to salam and sneeze during khutba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi positions on responding to greetings during the Friday khutba and apply the mufta bihi rule.

Core Idea
During the Friday khutba, verbal responses to greetings and sneezes are prohibited; respond silently in the heart.

This passage delves into the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding whether one may return a greeting (salam) or respond to a sneeze (tashmit) during the Friday sermon. Muhammad al-Shaybani prohibited it, Abu Yusuf allowed it, and Abu Hanifa taught silent internal response. Understanding this dispute refines your practice of adab during khutba.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (mufta bihi)
The relied-upon position is that verbal response is not allowed; one may respond silently in the heart.
This is the view of Muhammad in Al-Asl and the majority of later scholars.
2
Identify the opposing view within the madhhab
Abu Yusuf held that verbal response is permissible, based on practice recorded in his work Salat al-Athar.
This is not part of Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Abu Yusuf's proof is the established practice of early Muslims (the 'amal) who would return greetings during the khutba.
The text says 'they would return greetings and respond to the sneezer'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The response differentiates between immediate response and delayed response: Muhammad says respond after the khutba; Abu Yusuf says never respond. The text also records Abu Hanifa's silent internal response as the middle ground.
The author declares 'wa huwa al-sahih' for internal praise, indicating tarjih.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, the difference is whether one should verbally return a greeting during the khutba (Abu Yusuf) or only after (Muhammad) or silently (Abu Hanifa). The mufta bihi is silent internal response.
The practical effect: if you follow the mufta bihi, you do not speak, thus maintaining full attentiveness.

And Shams al-A'imma, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And here is another chapter, which is: proximity to the imam—is it better or distance from him? Many scholars said: distance is better so that one does not hear their praise and supplications, and the correct answer from our mashayikh, may Allah have mercy on them, is that proximity to him is better. When the imam comes out, they fold their scrolls and sit listening to the remembrance.' As in Al-Rawda and Jami' al-Kurdi. And in Khazanat al-Akmal: This is concerning the general public, because they were prevented from entering the maqsura; as for our time, there is no prevention in the first row that is next to the imam. And he does not return the greeting, nor does he respond to a sneeze. Thus said Muhammad in Al-Asl, and he did not mention any difference in it. And Muhammad narrated from Abu Yusuf in Salat al-Athar that they return greetings and respond to the sneezer. So it appears that what is in Al-Asl is the statement of Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, and the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in this is based on whether, if he does not return the greeting immediately, does he return it after the imam finishes the sermon? And from Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him: he praises Allah with his heart and does not move his lips, and that is correct as we have presented. Commentary
This passage covers two main issues. First, regarding whether it is better to sit close to the imam or far away during Friday prayer. Some scholars preferred distance to avoid hearing the imam's praise and supplications (which might lead to self-admiration), but the correct Hanafi view is that proximity is better, supported by hadith on the virtues of early attendance and closeness. The second issue is about responding to greetings (salam) and sneezes (tashmit) during the khutba. The base ruling from Muhammad al-Shaybani in Al-Asl is that one should not respond verbally. However, Abu Yusuf held that responding is permissible based on the practice of early Muslims recorded in his work Salat al-Athar. The dispute is analyzed: Muhammad says the response should be delayed until after the khutba, while Abu Yusuf says it should not be made even later. Abu Hanifa's position (outside the principal narrations) is to respond silently in the heart. For sneezing, the correct view is to praise Allah internally. The passage thus presents a detailed intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the permissibility of verbal responses during khutba.

الصلاة الأَثر (al-ṣalāt al-athar): A work by Abu Yusuf (likely a collection of hadith on prayer practices).

Responding to Greetings and Sneezes During the Friday Khutbah: Intra-Madhhab Differences
Intra-madhhab dispute on responding to greetings and sneezes during the Friday khutbah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on responding during the Friday khutbah and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
During the Friday khutbah, one must not respond verbally; the heart is sufficient.

The Friday khutbah is a time of attentive listening where speech is generally prohibited. But what about brief responses like returning a greeting or responding to a sneeze? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on this precise issue. In this lesson, we examine the rulings of the three main Hanafi imams and determine the operative position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and General Ruling
The act in question is any verbal response during the Friday khutbah, such as returning a greeting (salam) or saying 'yarhamukallah' to a sneeze. The general ruling is that such speech is prohibited.
The basis is the obligation to listen attentively to the khutbah.
2
State the Position of Zahir al-Riwayah (Imam Muhammad)
Imam Muhammad in al-Asl states that one does not respond at all. This is the primary source and carries significant authority.
Al-Asl is one of the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Contrast with the Narration from Abu Yusuf
In a narration from Abu Yusuf (in Salat al-Athar), it is reported that they did respond. This creates an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
This shows that the dispute is recorded in early Hanafi sources.
4
Understand the Key Disagreement: Timing of Response
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree on whether the response can be made after the khutbah. Muhammad says yes, Abu Yusuf says no. Additionally, Abu Hanifa (outside the transmitted sources) says respond only with the heart.
The disagreement is based on whether the prohibition extends beyond the khutbah.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The text states that the fatwa is on the view that if one hears himself (whispers), it is permissible, but the stronger view (al-aswab) is that he does not respond at all, and on this fatwa is given.
Thus the operative ruling is that one should not respond verbally, even after the khutbah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يَرُدُّ سلاماً، ولا يُشَمِّتُ عاطساً كذا قال محمد في «الأصل» ولم يذكر فيه خلافاً، وروى محمد عن أبي يوسف في صلاة الأثر أنهم يردون السلام ويشمتون العاطس، فتبين أن ما في الأصل قول محمد رحمه الله تعالى، والخلاف بين أبي يوسف ومحمد في هذا بناء على أنه إذا لم يرد السلام في الحال هل يرده بعدما فرغ الإمام من الخطبة؟ على قول محمد يرد، وعلى قول أبي يوسف لا يرد، وروي عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله في غير رواية الأصول يرد بقلبه، ولا يرد بلسانه، ولم يذكر محمد في «الأصل» أن العاطس هل يحمد الله تعالى؟ ذكر الحسن بن زياد عن أبي حنيفة يحمد في نفسه ولا يجهر، وهذا صحيح، وعن محمد رحمه الله تعالى يحمد الله بقلبه ولا يحرك شفتيه، وهو الصحيح كما قدمناه، وفي «النصاب»: إذا شمت أو رد السلام في صلاته حتى يفرغ من صلاته. يسمع نفسه جاز، وعليه الفتوى. وفي الكبرى الأصوب أنه لا يجيب وبه يفتى، وفي «المحيط»: إذا فرغ من الخطبة يحمد الله تعالى بلسانه، وهذا كما لو سمع النداء وهو في الخلاء يجيب بقلبه، وإذا فرغ يجيب بلسانه، وفي «الحجة»: كان أبو حنيفة رحمه الله يكره تشميت العاطس ورد السلام إذا خرج الإمام فلا يفعله، ولا يصلي نافلة، ولا يتكلم حتى يفرغ من صلاته) لما قدمناه، وليس من ذلك ما لو خاف على إنسان الوقوع في بئر ونحوه، أو عقرباً تدب عليه فإنه يحذره، لأنه حق آدمي، والإنصات حق الله فيقدم الآدمي لحاجته. فإن قيل: جاء في الحديث أن الدعاء مستجاب وقت الإقامة في يوم الجمعة، فكيف يسكت عند أبي حنيفة؟ قلنا: يدعو بقلبه لا بلسانه كما في «الدراية».
He does not return greetings, nor does he respond to a sneeze. Thus said Muhammad in al-Asl, and he did not mention any disagreement in it. And Muhammad narrated from Abu Yusuf in Salat al-Athar that they return greetings and respond to sneezes. Thus it became clear that what is in al-Asl is the statement of Muhammad. The disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in this is based on whether, if one does not return the greeting immediately, he returns it after the imam finishes the khutbah? According to Muhammad's statement, he returns; according to Abu Yusuf's statement, he does not return. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa, outside the transmitted sources, that he returns with his heart and does not return with his tongue. And Muhammad did not mention in al-Asl whether the one who sneezes praises Allah. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa that he praises within himself and does not do so aloud, and this is correct. And from Muhammad, he praises Allah with his heart and does not move his lips, and that is the correct view as we have stated. And in al-Nisab: If he responds to a sneeze or returns a greeting during his prayer until he finishes his prayer. He hears himself, it is permissible, and on this is the fatwa. And in al-Kubra, the correct view is that he does not respond, and on this fatwa is given. And in al-Muhit: When he finishes the khutbah, he praises Allah with his tongue, and this is like when he hears the call while in the lavatory, he responds with his heart, and when he finishes, he responds with his tongue. And in al-Hujja: Abu Hanifa used to dislike responding to a sneeze and returning greetings when the imam had come out, so he would not do it, nor would he pray supererogatory prayers, nor speak until he finished his prayer. And this does not include if he fears that someone might fall into a well or the like, or a scorpion creeping on him; then he warns him, because it is a human right, and listening silently is a divine right, so the human right is given precedence due to need. If it is said: A hadith states that supplication is answered at the time of the iqama on Friday; so why does Abu Hanifa remain silent? We say: He makes supplication with his heart, not with his tongue, as in al-Diraya.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah discusses the rulings on responding to greetings (salam) and responding to a sneeze (tashmit) during the Friday khutbah. The author begins by stating the general rule: the listener should not respond verbally. He then reports the positions of the Hanafi imams. Imam Muhammad's position in al-Asl is that one does not respond at all. However, a narration from Abu Yusuf indicates that they did respond, leading to an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf. The key point of disagreement is whether the response can be made after the khutbah ends. Muhammad says yes, Abu Yusuf says no. A narration from Abu Hanifa says that one responds only with the heart, not verbally. As for praising Allah after sneezing, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad both agree it is done silently, without moving the lips. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that if one can hear himself (i.e., speak quietly), it is permissible to respond, but the better view (al-aswab) is that he does not respond at all. The passage also clarifies an exception: if someone is in danger, one may speak to warn them, because saving a life is a human right that takes precedence over the divine right of attentive listening. Finally, a potential objection about the virtue of supplication during the iqama is answered: one supplicates silently in the heart.

Key Terms
يُشَمِّتُ عاطساً (yushammitu 'ātisan): To respond to a sneeze by saying 'yarhamukallah' (may Allah have mercy on you).
الأصل (al-Aṣl): One of the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, containing foundational Hanafi rulings.
صلاة الأثر (Ṣalāt al-Athar): A transmission or work by Imam Abu Yusuf, possibly referring to the book 'Kitab al-Athar' which contains narrations from the Prophet and the Companions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Respond with the tongue.
B) Respond with the heart only.
C) Respond after the khutbah.
D) Do not respond at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab strictly prohibits any verbal response during the Friday khutbah, with silent heart response being permissible only as an exception.

Prohibition of Speech and Greeting During Friday Khutbah: Hanafi Position and Dispute
Explains Hanafi rulings on eating/drinking during khutbah and the imam not greeting the congregation, with inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on eating and greeting during Friday khutbah and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument regarding the khatib's greeting.

Core Idea
During Friday khutbah, even voluntary speech and greeting are prohibited, akin to prayer.

This lesson examines two key rulings during the Friday khutbah: the prohibition of eating, drinking, and idle actions for the listeners, and the rule that the khatib (preacher) should not greet the congregation upon ascending the minbar. The Hanafi school bases these rulings on the principle that the khutbah session is akin to prayer in its sanctity. A notable disagreement with the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools on the greeting issue provides an opportunity to understand how different usul lead to different fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two rulings: for the listener, eating/drinking/talking are haram (stronger view) or makruh; for the khatib, he should not greet the congregation upon ascending the minbar.
The stronger view from al-Kamal declares all speech, eating, drinking, writing as haram.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad hold that the khatib should greet the congregation when he ascends the minbar, considering it sunnah.
Malik agrees with the Hanafi position.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent cites a hadith from Ibn Umar that the Prophet would greet when ascending the minbar.
The Hanafi school questions the strength of this hadith.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the general prohibition 'When the imam comes out, there is no prayer and no speech' overrides the Ibn Umar hadith, which may be abrogated or weak (as per al-Bayhaqi). Also, greeting forces a prohibited response.
The principle of avoiding anything that leads to prohibited speech (sadd al-dhara'i) is applied.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In the Hanafi school, if the khatib greets, it is considered makruh (disliked); in the Shafi'i school, it is sunnah and recommended.
A Hanafi follower would remain silent during the greeting, not responding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره الحاضر الخطبة الأكل والشرب، والعَبَث، والالتفات. ولا يسلم الخطيب على القوم إذا استوى على المنبر. بل صرح الكمال بالحرمة، فقال: يحرم في الخطبة الكلام وإن كان أمراً بمعروف أو تسبيحاً، والأكل والشرب والكتابة انتهى، أي: إذا كان الكاتب يسمع لما قدمناه عنه إذ كتابة من لا يسمع الخطبة غير ممتنعة. (وَ) كره (العَبَث، والالتفات) فيجتنب الحاضر وقت الخطبة ما يجتنبه في الصلاة، كما في مجمع الروايات، وإذا احتبى الرجل في حالة الخطبة لا بأس به، لكن لا يضع جبهته على ركبته، لأن السنة هي المواجهة ولأنه يورث النوم، كذا في «التجنيس»، ولا يسلّم الخطيب على القوم إذا استوى على المنبر)؛ لأنه يلجئهم إلى ما نـهو عنه، قال شيخ مشايخنا العلامة نور الدين الشيخ علي المقدسي رحمهم الله في شرحه «نظم الكنز»: وأما الخطيب فيشترط أن يتأهب للإمامة في الجمعة والسنة الطهارة والقيام واستقبال القوم وترك الكلام والسلام إلى دخوله في الصلاة كذا في المجتبى، فما ذكره «الحدادي» ومن حذا حذوه من أنه يسلّم إذا صعد وأقبل غير مقبول، انتهى. قلت: وقد نقل في «الدراية» كلام المجتبى إلى أن قال: وترك السلام من خروجه إلى دخوله في الصلاة وترك الكلام، وبه قال مالك، وقال الشافعي وأحمد إذا صعد المنبر: السنة أن يسلم على القوم إذا قابلهم بوجهه، كذا روى ابن عمر عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، والحجة عليه قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «إِذَا خَرَجَ الإِمَامُ فَلَا صَلاةَ ولا كلام»، وما رواه يحتمل أن يكون قبل هذا القول، مع أن البيهقي قال: ليس بقوي.
And it is disliked for the attendee of the khutbah to eat, drink, play, and turn away. And the khatib does not greet the people when he ascends the minbar. Rather, al-Kamal explicitly stated prohibition, saying: Talking during the khutbah is unlawful, even if it is commanding good or tasbih, and eating, drinking, and writing. End quote. Meaning if the writer hears, because one who does not hear the khutbah is not prevented from writing. And it is disliked to play and turn away, so the attendee avoids during the time of the khutbah what he avoids in prayer, as in Majma' al-Riwayat. If a man sits with his knees drawn up during the khutbah, there is no harm, but he should not place his forehead on his knee, because the sunnah is to face forward and because it induces sleep. And the khatib does not greet the people when he ascends the minbar, because it would force them to do what they are prohibited from. The teacher of our teachers, al-Allama Nur al-Din al-Shaykh Ali al-Maqdisi, said in his commentary on Nazm al-Kanz: The khatib must prepare for the imamate in Jumu'ah; the sunnah is purity, standing, facing the people, and refraining from speech and greeting until he enters into the prayer. So what al-Hadadi and those who follow him said—that he greets when he ascends and turns around—is unacceptable. End quote. I say: And in al-Diraya, the statement of al-Mujtaba is quoted to the effect: and refraining from greeting from his leaving until his entering into prayer, and refraining from speech. Malik said the same. Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said: When he ascends the minbar, the sunnah is to greet the people when he faces them, as Ibn Umar narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him). The proof against him is his statement (peace be upon him): 'When the imam comes out, there is no prayer and no speech.' What he narrated is likely to be before this statement, and al-Bayhaqi said it is not strong.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi rulings on conduct during the Friday khutbah. It begins with a general ruling that eating, drinking, playing, and turning away are disliked (makruh). However, the commentator notes that al-Kamal explicitly stated that these are unlawful (haram), including any speech, even if it is commanding good or tasbih. The key principle is that the listener should behave as if in prayer: avoiding all distractions. An exception is made for sitting with knees drawn (ihtiba) as long as one does not lower the head onto the knees, because that leads to sleep. The second major ruling concerns the khatib (preacher): he should not greet the congregation when he ascends the minbar, because it would oblige them to respond, which is prohibited. The commentator supports this by quoting al-Mujtaba and stating that the khatib should refrain from speech and greeting from the moment he leaves his place until he enters the prayer. A disagreement is noted with al-Hadadi who allowed greeting. Then an inter-madhhab dispute is presented: Imam Malik agrees with the Hanafi position, while Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad hold that the khatib should greet the people when he faces them, citing a hadith from Ibn Umar. The Hanafi response is that the general prohibition 'When the imam comes out, there is no prayer and no speech' overrides that hadith, and the narration from Ibn Umar may have been abrogated or is weak according to al-Bayhaqi.

Key Terms
الخطيب (al-khaṭīb): The person delivering the Friday khutbah sermon.
العَبَث (al-'abath): Fidgeting or playing with hands/objects during the khutbah.
المقصورة (al-maqṣūrah): A private enclosure near the mihrab where the ruler or imam often prays. Referenced in the earlier discussion about sitting near the imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the khatib greeting the congregation when he ascends the minbar?
A) It is sunnah (recommended).
B) It is wajib (obligatory).
C) It is not done and is disliked (makruh).
D) It is permissible (mubah).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats the Friday khutbah with the same sanctity as prayer, prohibiting all speech and greetings, and differing from the Shafi'i school on the khatib's greeting.

Salah — Prayer

Rulings on Disliked Acts During Friday Khutbah and the Khatib's Greeting
State prohibitions for audience during khutbah and ruling on khatib's greeting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on prohibited and disliked acts during the Friday khutbah and explain why the khatib should not greet the congregation.

Core Idea
During the khutbah, silence is wajib; the khatib must avoid greeting to prevent compelling the audience to sin.

In this passage from Imdad al-Fattah, we examine the conduct required of both the congregation and the khatib during the Friday khutbah. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the khutbah is akin to prayer in its sanctity, requiring attentive silence and prohibition of distracting actions. We will also explore a key inter-madhhab dispute: whether the khatib should greet the audience upon ascending the pulpit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited acts for the audience
Locate the specific actions the text declares disliked (makruh) or prohibited (haram) during the khutbah: eating, drinking, idle play (al-'abath), turning (al-iltifat), and speech.
Note that al-Kamal explicitly declares speech haram, and the relied-upon position treats the others as makruh tahrimi.
2
Understand the proof and exceptions
The proof is the hadith: 'When the imam comes out, there is no prayer and no speech.' Note the exception for warning of danger (e.g., a scorpion) because it is a human right that takes precedence over the right of Allah to silence.
Also note that one may supplicate silently in the heart, not aloud.
3
Analyze the khatib's greeting dispute
Contrast the Hanafi position (no greeting) with the Shafi'i/Hanbali view (sunnah to greet). Identify the proofs: Hanafis cite the same hadith and consider the opposing hadith weak or abrogated.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in Hanafi and Maliki practice, the khatib does not greet; in Shafi'i and Hanbali, he does.
4
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
Consider a situation where someone enters the mosque and the khatib has already begun. According to the Hanafi ruling, the latecomer should not engage in supererogatory prayer or greet others; he should sit and listen silently.
If a person is in danger, however, he must warn him, even if it means speaking.

(وَ) كره (العَبَث، والالتفات) فيجتنب الحاضر وقت الخطبة ما يجتنبه في الصلاة، كما في مجمع الروايات ، وإذا احتبى الرجل في حالة الخطبة لا بأس به، لكن لا يضع جبهته على ركبته، لأن السنة هي المواجهة ولأنه يورث النوم، كذا في « التجنيس»، ولا يسلّم الخطيب على القوم إذا استوى على المنبر )؛ لأنه يلجئهم إلى مـا نـهـو عنه (۳) ، قال شيخ مشايخنا العلامة نور الدين الشيخ علي المقدسي » رحمهم الله في شرحه « نظم الكنز » : وأما الخطيب فيشترط أن يتأهب للإمامة في الجمعة والسنة الطهارة والقيام واستقبال القوم وترك الكلام والسلام إلى دخوله في الصلاة كذا في المجتبى ، فما ذكره « الحدادي» ومـن حـذا حذوه من أنه يسلّم إذا صعد وأقبل غير مقبول، انتهى. قلت: وقد نقل في « الدراية» كــلام المجتبى» إلى أن قال: وترك السلام من خروجه إلى دخوله في الصلاة وترك الكلام، وبه قال مالك، وقال الشافعي وأحمد إذا صعد المنبر : السنة أن يسلم على القوم إذا قابلهم بوجهه، كذا روى ابن عمر عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، والحجة عليه قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « إِذَا خَرَجَ الإِمَامُ فَلَا صَلاةَ ولا كلام ، وما رواه يحتمل أن يكون قبل هذا القول، مع أن البيهقي قال: ليس بقوي. به
Until he finishes his prayer. And it is disliked for the one present at the khutbah to eat, drink, engage in idle play, and turn. And the khatib should not greet the people when he ascends the pulpit. If he hears himself, it is permissible, and upon it is the fatwa. In al-Kubra, the more correct is that he does not respond, and upon it is the fatwa. And in al-Muhit: When he finishes the khutbah, he praises Allah (ta'ala) with his tongue. And this is like if he hears the call while in the bathroom, he responds with his heart, and when he finishes, he responds with his tongue. And in al-Hujjah: Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) disliked sneezing praise and returning greetings when the imam has come out, so he does not do it, nor does he pray supererogatory prayers, nor does he speak until he finishes his prayer, due to what we have presented. And from that is not the case if he fears that a person will fall into a well or the like, or a scorpion crawling on him; then he warns him, because it is a human right, and listening silently is the right of Allah, so the human right is given precedence due to need. If it is said: A hadith came that supplication is answered at the time of the iqamah on Friday, so how can one be silent according to Abu Hanifa? We say: He supplicates with his heart, not with his tongue, as in al-Dirayah. And it is disliked for the one present at the khutbah to eat and drink. Rather, al-Kamal explicitly stated prohibition, saying: Speech is prohibited during the khutbah even if it is commanding good or glorifying [Allah], and eating, drinking, and writing. End of quote, i.e., if the writer hears due to what we have presented about him, because writing by one who is not [listening] to the khutbah is not prevented. And it is disliked to engage in idle play and turning. So the one present, during the time of the khutbah, avoids what he avoids in prayer, as in Majma' al-Riwayat. And if a man sits hugging his knees during the khutbah, there is no harm, but he should not place his forehead on his knee because the sunnah is facing [the khatib] and because it induces sleep. The teacher of our scholars, the eminent scholar Nur al-Din al-Shaykh 'Ali al-Maqdisi (may Allah have mercy on them), said in his commentary on Nazm al-Kanz: "As for the khatib, it is required that he prepare for imamate in Jumu'ah, and the sunnah is purity, standing, facing the people, and refraining from speech and greeting until he enters into the prayer." Thus in al-Mujtaba. So what al-Hadadi and those who follow him mentioned—that he greets when he ascends and faces—is not accepted. End. I say: It has been reported in al-Dirayah the statement of al-Mujtaba until he said: "And refraining from greeting from his exit until his entry into the prayer, and refraining from speech." And Malik held this view. Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said: when he ascends the pulpit, the sunnah is to greet the people when he faces them. As such, Ibn 'Umar reported from the Prophet (peace be upon him). The proof against him is his saying (peace be upon him): "When the imam comes out, there is no prayer and no speech." And what he reported may have been before this statement, especially since al-Bayhaqi said: It is not strong.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah discusses the conduct of both the audience and the khatib during the Friday khutbah. It begins by stating that it is disliked (makruh) for those present to eat, drink, engage in idle play, or turn away during the khutbah. The author then notes that there is a difference of opinion on whether one should respond verbally to the adhan or praise if they hear it during the khutbah; the relied-upon position is that one should respond inwardly, not verbally. The passage then elaborates that according to Abu Hanifa, even responding to a sneeze or returning greetings is disliked once the imam has come out, and one should not engage in supererogatory prayers or speech until the prayer is finished. However, an exception is made for urgent matters like warning someone of danger, as human rights take precedence over the right of Allah to silence. The text then addresses a potential objection: if supplication is answered at the time of iqamah on Friday, how can one be silent? The answer is that one supplicates silently in the heart. The author then affirms that eating and drinking are disliked, with some scholars holding that they are actually forbidden (haram) during the khutbah. The passage further explains that idle play and turning are likewise disliked, and the audience should observe the same decorum as in prayer. Finally, the ruling on the khatib greeting is examined: the Hanafi position (also held by Malik) is that the khatib should not greet the people when he ascends the pulpit, as it would force them to respond, which they are forbidden from doing. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools consider it sunnah to greet. The proof for the Hanafi position is the hadith 'When the imam comes out, there is no prayer and no speech,' and the contrary report from Ibn 'Umar is considered weak or abrogated.

Key Terms
الخُطبة (al-khutbah): The sermon delivered by the imam on Friday before the prayer.
العَبَث (al-'abath): Idle play or fidgeting with hands or objects during the khutbah.
الإنصات (al-insat): Listening attentively and silently to the khutbah.
تشميت العاطس (tashmit al-'atis): Responding to a sneeze by saying 'yarhamukallah'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is sunnah to greet.
B) It is makruh to greet.
C) He should not greet.
D) He must greet.

Key Takeaway
The congregation must remain silent and avoid all distracting actions during the Friday khutbah, and the khatib must not greet to avoid compelling them to sin.

Leaving the City After the Adhan and the Status of Zuhr Before Jumu'ah
State prohibition of leaving after adhan and effect of praying Zuhr before Jumu'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on leaving the city after the Friday adhan and explain the effect of praying Zuhr before Jumu'ah, including when it becomes void.

Core Idea
Proceeding to Jumu'ah invalidates a prior Zuhr in the Hanafi school, even if Jumu'ah is missed.

This lesson explores the interplay between the obligation of Jumu'ah and the optional or forbidden act of praying Zuhr beforehand. The source passage from Imdad al-Fattah clarifies the conditions under which leaving the city is disliked, and the precise moment a premature Zuhr becomes void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that leaving the city after the adhan is makruh tahrimi, and praying Zuhr without excuse is haram.
The command 'fas'aw' (proceed) in Surah al-Jumu'ah is the basis.
2
Understand suspended validity (in'iqad mawqufan)
A Zuhr prayed before Jumu'ah by an obligated person is valid but suspended; it awaits the decision to attend Jumu'ah.
If he does not attend, the Zuhr remains valid. If he proceeds, it becomes void.
3
Determine when Zuhr becomes void
According to Abu Hanifa, voidance occurs upon setting out (sa'y) from one's house, not upon joining the prayer.
The condition is departing from the home; steps within a spacious house do not count.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that Zuhr remains valid until one enters the congregation or completes Jumu'ah.
Their proof is that sa'y alone is not equivalent to a pillar; the actual act of prayer is stronger.
5
Note the precautionary principle (ihtiyat)
Abu Hanifa views sa'y as a characteristic specific to Jumu'ah, so engaging in it is like engaging in a pillar; the stronger prayer (Jumu'ah) commands caution over the weaker (Zuhr).
This is why Zuhr is voided even if Jumu'ah is not caught.

Source Text

وكرة الخروج منَ المِصْرِ، بعدَ النَّداءِ مَا لَمْ يُصَلِّ وَمَنْ لَا جُمُعَةَ عَلَيْهِ إِنْ أَدَّاهَا جَازَ عَنْ فَرْضِ الوَقْتِ، وَمَنْ لَا عُدْرَ لَهُ لَوْ صَلَّى الظُّهْرَ قَبْلَهَا حَرُمَ، فَإِنْ سَعَى إِلَيْهَا ، والإمَامُ فِيهَا بَطَلَ ظُهْرُهُ، وإنْ لَمْ يُدْرِكْهَا . قال عبد الحق في الأحكام الكبرى هو مرسل، وليس بحجة عنده، وإن أسنده أحمد من حديث عبـد بن لهيعة، وهو معروف في الضعفاء، فلا يحتج به انتهى وكره لمن تجب عليه الجمعة ( الخروج منَ المِصْرِ ) يوم الجمعة ( بعد النّداءِ ) أي: الأذان الأول، وقيل: المعتـبر الثـانـي (مـا لـم يُصَلِّ) الجمعة، لأنه شمله الأمر بالسعي إلى الجمعة، ولم يصر مسافراً قبل الخروج، ولا منفصلاً عن المصر، وإذا خرج قبل الزوال فلا بأس به بلا خلاف كما في ( التاتارخانية»، وكذا بعد فراغ الجمعة وإن لم يدركها (وَمَنْ لا جُمُعَةَ عَلَيْهِ) كمريض ومسافر ورقيق وامرأة وأعمى ومقعد ( إن أَدَّاهَا جَازَ عَنْ فَرْض الوَقْتِ)؛ لأن السقوط تخفيفاً للعذر ، فإذا تحمل ما لم يكلف بـه وهـو الجمعة، جاز عن فرض الوقت وهو الظهر، كالمسافر إذا صام، وكلام الشراح يدل على أن الأفضل لهم الجمعة لقولهم: إن الظهر لهم يوم الجمعة رخصة فدل على أن العزيمة صلاة الجمعة، ويستثنى منهم المرأة لأنها ممنوعة عن حضور الجماعات. (وَمَنْ لا عُدْرَ لَهُ) يمنعه عن حضور الجمعة (لَوْ صَلَّى الظُّهْرَ قَبْلَهَا ) أي : قبل صلاة الجمعة انعقد ظهره لوجود وقت أصل الفرض، وهـو الـظـهـر فـي حـق الكافة، إلا أنه لما كان مأموراً بإسقاطه بالجمعة (حَرُمَ) عليه فعل الأصل، وكان انعقاده موقوفاً ( فَإِنْ سَعَى) أي: مشى لا مسرعاً ( إلَيْهَا ) أي : إلى الجمعة، (وَ) كان الإمَامُ فِيهَا) أي: صلاة الجمعة لم يتمها إذ ذاك أو أقيمت بعدما سعى إليها ( بَطَلَ ظُهْرُهُ) أي: بطل وصفه وصـار نـفـلاً، وكذا حكـم المعذور لو صلى الظهر، ثم سعى إلى الجمعة بطل ظهره، وإنْ لَمْ يُدْرِكْهَا)، وهذا عند «أبـي حنيفة» على تخريج البلخيين، وهو الأصح، والمعتبر في السعي الانفصال عن داره، فلا يبطل ظهره قبله على المختار. وقيل: إذا خطا خطوتين في البيت الواسع يبطل، ولا يبطل إذا كان السعي مقارناً للفراغ منها أو بعدها، أو لم تقم الجمعة ؟ قالا : لا يبطل ظهره حتى يدخل مع القوم، وفي رواية حتى يتمها، حتى لو أفسدها بعدما شرع فيها لا يبطل ظهره على هذه الرواية، لهما أن السعي إلى الجمعة دون الظهر فلا يبطل به الظهر، والجمعة فوقه فيبطل بها، ولأبي حنيفة رحمه الله : أن السعي إلى الجمعة من خصائصها، فصار الاشتغال به كالاشتغال بركن من أركانها بجامع الاختصاص، فيؤثر في ارتفاض الظهر احتياطاً، إذ الأقوى يحتاط لإثباتـه مـا لا يحتاط لإثبات الأضعف، ولو صلى مسافر الظهر إماماً، ثم حضر الجمعة فصلاها فهي فرضه، وجازت صلاة أولئك، ولو قدمه الإمام لسبق حدث جازت صلاة القوم؛ لأنَّ ظهره ارتفض في حقه دون أولئك الذي صلى
And it is disliked to leave the city after the call until he prays. And whoever is not obligated to attend Jumu'ah, if he performs it, it suffices for the obligatory prayer of the time. And whoever has no excuse, if he prays Zuhr before it, it is forbidden. Then if he proceeds to it while the imam is in it, his Zuhr becomes void, even if he does not catch it. 'Abd al-Haqq said in al-Ahkam al-Kubra: It is mursal and not a proof according to him, and if Ahmad narrated it from 'Abd ibn Lahi'a, he is known among the weak, so it is not used as proof. End. And it is disliked for one upon whom Jumu'ah is obligatory to leave the city on Friday after the call, i.e., the first adhan, and it is said the second is considered, until he prays Jumu'ah, because the command to proceed to Jumu'ah includes him, and he does not become a traveler before leaving, nor separated from the city. And if he leaves before zawal (noon), there is no harm without dispute, as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. Similarly after finishing Jumu'ah, even if he did not catch it. And whoever is not obligated for Jumu'ah, such as a sick person, traveler, slave, woman, blind person, and permanently disabled, if he performs it, it suffices for the obligatory prayer of the time, because the obligation is dropped as a concession for the excuse. So if he undertakes what he was not obliged to do, i.e., Jumu'ah, it suffices for the obligatory time, which is Zuhr, like a traveler who fasts. And the words of the commentators indicate that Jumu'ah is better for them because they say: Zuhr for them on Friday is a concession, so the determination (azimah) is the Jumu'ah prayer. However, women are excepted because they are prohibited from attending congregations. And whoever has no excuse preventing him from attending Jumu'ah, if he prays Zuhr before it, i.e., before the Jumu'ah prayer, his Zuhr is valid because the time for the original obligation exists, which is Zuhr for all people. But since he is commanded to drop it by means of Jumu'ah, it is forbidden for him to perform the original act, and its validity is suspended. Then if he proceeds (i.e., walks, not rushing) to it (Jumu'ah), and the imam is in it (i.e., the Jumu'ah prayer has not yet been completed, or it was established after he proceeded), his Zuhr becomes void, i.e., its validity is void and it becomes supererogatory. Similarly for the excused person: if he prayed Zuhr, then proceeded to Jumu'ah, his Zuhr becomes void, even if he does not catch it. This is according to Abu Hanifa on the inference of the Balkh scholars, and it is the most correct. And what is considered in proceeding is departing from his house; so his Zuhr does not become void before that, according to the chosen position. And it is said: if he takes two steps in a spacious house, it becomes void. And it does not become void if the proceeding is simultaneous with finishing it or after, or if Jumu'ah was not established. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: his Zuhr does not become void until he enters with the congregation. And in one narration: until he completes it, such that even if he invalidates it after starting, his Zuhr does not become void according to this narration. For them, proceeding to Jumu'ah is not the same as Zuhr, so it does not invalidate Zuhr, and Jumu'ah is superior, so it invalidates it. For Abu Hanifa: proceeding to Jumu'ah is one of its characteristics, so engaging in it is like engaging in one of its pillars due to the commonality of exclusivity, thus it affects the invalidation of Zuhr as a precaution, because the stronger is more cautious in establishing itself than in establishing the weaker. And if a traveler prays Zuhr as an imam, then attends Jumu'ah and prays it, it is his obligatory prayer, and the prayer of those [who prayed behind him] is valid. And if the imam steps forward due to an event, the people's prayer is valid because his Zuhr became void in his case but not for those who prayed behind him.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah deals with two interconnected rulings: (1) the prohibition of leaving the city after the Friday adhan for those obligated to attend, and (2) the effect of praying Zuhr before Jumu'ah for both those obligated and those not. The author begins by stating that it is disliked (makruh) to leave the city after the adhan (whether the first or second, depending on the view) on Friday until one has prayed Jumu'ah. This is because one is commanded to proceed to the prayer. However, leaving before zawal (noon) is permissible without dispute, as is leaving after completing Jumu'ah or even if one missed it. The text then turns to those not obligated for Jumu'ah (e.g., sick, traveler, slave, woman, blind, disabled). If they choose to perform Jumu'ah, it counts as their obligatory prayer for that time (i.e., Zuhr), making it better for them to do so since it is the azimah (determination). Women are excepted because they are prohibited from attending congregational prayers. The core of the lesson is about someone who is obligated but has no excuse—he is forbidden from praying Zuhr before Jumu'ah, and if he does so, his prayer is valid but suspended. Then, if he subsequently proceeds to Jumu'ah (by walking, not rushing) while the imam is still in the Jumu'ah prayer, his Zuhr becomes void and turns into a supererogatory (nafl) prayer. This is the position of Abu Hanifa, considered most correct, and it applies even if the person does not actually catch the Jumu'ah. The condition for voiding is that he departs from his house; steps within a spacious house do not count according to the chosen view. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that his Zuhr does not become void until he actually joins the congregation or completes the Jumu'ah prayer. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that proceeding to Jumu'ah is a unique characteristic (khassa) of the Jumu'ah prayer, so engaging in it is akin to performing a pillar of Jumu'ah, which necessitates the invalidation of the Zuhr as a precaution (ihtiyat). The passage concludes with a case of a traveler who leads a Zuhr prayer as imam and then attends Jumu'ah; his Jumu'ah is his obligatory prayer, and the prayer of those who prayed Zuhr behind him remains valid because his Zuhr was voided only for himself.

Key Terms
المِصْر (al-misr): The city or town where Friday prayer is established.
السَّعْي (al-sa'y): Proceeding or walking to the Friday prayer, not rushing.
الانعقاد موقوفاً (al-in'iqaad mawqufan): A prayer that is valid but its status is suspended pending subsequent action (attending Jumu'ah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens to the Zuhr prayer of a person who prays it before Jumu'ah and then proceeds to Jumu'ah but does not catch it?
A) His Zuhr remains valid as obligatory.
B) His Zuhr becomes void and becomes nafl (supererogatory).
C) His Zuhr remains valid but he must repeat it.
D) His Zuhr becomes void and he must repeat Zuhr.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that once a person sets out to attend Jumu'ah, any prior Zuhr prayer becomes void, even if Jumu'ah is not caught.
